What Is Kalam?

The Sixth Chapter and Its Four Topics
Author introduces the sixth chapter on sam‘iyyat and enumerates four topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the sixth chapter on sam‘iyyāt and the four major topics it covers.

Core Idea
The sixth chapter covers the revealed truths known through hearing the prophets.

The author now turns to the third major division of theology: the sam‘iyyāt, or revealed matters. This chapter is not a single argument but a collection of four foundational topics that every Muslim must understand. The passage lists them clearly, setting the stage for detailed discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter's subject
Read the first sentence to see what 'al-sam‘iyyāt' refers to. Note that it is the sixth chapter.
Sam‘iyyāt literally means 'audible matters', but here it means doctrines known through revelation.
2
List the four topics
The passage explicitly names four topics. Write them down: prophethood, resurrection, names and judgments, imamate.
3
Understand the significance
These four topics cover the core of Islamic eschatology, prophethood, and leadership. They are interdependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المقصد السادس في السمعيات وفيه فصول. أربعة مباحث : النبوة، ومباحث المعاد، ومباحث الأسماء والأحكام وما يلائمها، ومباحث الإمامة. (قوله : الفصل الأول في النبوة وفيه مباحث) . وهو كون الإنسان مبعوثاً من الحق إلى الخلق. فإن كان النبي مأخوذاً من النباوة وهو الارتفاع لعلو شأنه واشتهار مكانه أو من النبى بمعنى الطريق لكونه وسيلة إلى الحق تعالى، فالنبوة على الأصل كالأبوة، وإن كان من النبأ وهو الخبر لإنبائه عن الله تعالى فعلى قلب الهمزة واواً ثم الإدغام كالمروة .
He said: The sixth chapter is on al-sam‘iyyāt (audible matters), and it contains sections. Four topics: prophethood, topics on the resurrection, topics on names and judgments and what accords with them, and topics on the imamate. (His words: The first chapter is on prophethood, and it contains investigations). And it (prophethood) is a human being being sent from the Truth to the creation. So if the word nabī is taken from nabāwah, which is elevation due to high status and fame, or from al-nabī meaning the path because he is a means to the Truth, Exalted is He, then nubuwwa on the pattern of ubūwa; and if it is from al-naba’ meaning the news because he gives news about God, Exalted is He, then the hamza is changed to a wāw and then assimilated, like marwa.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the sixth chapter on al-sam‘iyyāt (the audible matters), which are the revealed truths. The author first states that this chapter contains sections, and then lists four major topics: prophethood, the resurrection, names and judgments (i.e., issues of faith and sin), and the imamate. He then begins the first chapter on prophethood. He gives a linguistic definition of nubuwwa: it means that a human being is sent from God to the creation. He then discusses the etymology of the word nabī. Nabī could be derived from nabāwah, meaning height or elevation, referring to the prophet's high status and fame; or from nabī meaning a path, because the prophet is a means to God; in that case, the word nubuwwa follows the pattern of ‘ubūwa. Alternatively, it could be derived from naba’, meaning a report, because the prophet reports from God; then the original hamza becomes wāw and is assimilated, as in the word marwa. Thus the definition is presented with linguistic analysis.

Key Terms
السمعيات
النبوة
المعاد
الأسماء والأحكام
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think sam‘iyyāt only refers to sounds or auditory knowledge.
Correction: In kalam, sam‘iyyāt are doctrines known through revelation, not through reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many major topics (مباحث) does the sixth chapter contain?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The sixth chapter on sam‘iyyāt is organized around four essential topics: prophethood, resurrection, names and judgments, and imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The chapter lists prophethood as the first of the four topics. Why do you think the author placed it first? How does this order affect your personal priority in seeking knowledge about prophetic teachings?
?
You have just learned that 'sam‘iyyāt' means matters known through revelation. Are you treating the Quran and Sunnah as the ultimate source for your beliefs about prophethood, resurrection, and imamate, or do you rely more on your own opinions?
?
The topic of 'names and judgments' includes what accords with them. This means your understanding of God's names impacts your judgments in life. Examine yourself: how does your knowledge of Allah’s names shape your daily decisions and actions?

Linguistic Definition of Nubuwwa
Author linguistically defines nubuwwa and discusses etymological roots of 'nabī'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the word 'nabī' and the concept of nubuwwa as being sent from God.

Core Idea
The word 'nabī' can mean 'elevated one', 'path', or 'reporter from God'.

Having introduced the chapter, the author now defines nubuwwa linguistically. This is not a theological definition yet, but an exploration of the Arabic word's roots. The analysis shows how the term carries connotations of loftiness, guidance, and communication.

Guided Path
1
Define nubuwwa conceptually
Read the sentence: 'وهو كون الإنسان مبعوثاً من الحق إلى الخلق'. That is the core meaning: a human messenger sent by God to creation.
2
Examine the first two derivations
The author says nabī may come from nabāwah (elevation) or from nabī (path). Note the pattern nubuwwa follows like ubūwa.
3
Examine the third derivation
Alternatively, nabī comes from naba’ (news). The hamza changes to wāw and assimilates.
This is a common linguistic rule in Arabic.
4
Synthesize the meanings
Think about how all three roots contribute to the full concept of prophethood: elevated status, being a means to God, and conveying news from Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المقصد السادس في السمعيات وفيه فصول. أربعة مباحث : النبوة، ومباحث المعاد، ومباحث الأسماء والأحكام وما يلائمها، ومباحث الإمامة. (قوله : الفصل الأول في النبوة وفيه مباحث) . وهو كون الإنسان مبعوثاً من الحق إلى الخلق. فإن كان النبي مأخوذاً من النباوة وهو الارتفاع لعلو شأنه واشتهار مكانه أو من النبى بمعنى الطريق لكونه وسيلة إلى الحق تعالى، فالنبوة على الأصل كالأبوة، وإن كان من النبأ وهو الخبر لإنبائه عن الله تعالى فعلى قلب الهمزة واواً ثم الإدغام كالمروة .
He said: The sixth chapter is on al-sam‘iyyāt (audible matters), and it contains sections. Four topics: prophethood, topics on the resurrection, topics on names and judgments and what accords with them, and topics on the imamate. (His words: The first chapter is on prophethood, and it contains investigations). And it (prophethood) is a human being being sent from the Truth to the creation. So if the word nabī is taken from nabāwah, which is elevation due to high status and fame, or from al-nabī meaning the path because he is a means to the Truth, Exalted is He, then nubuwwa on the pattern of ubūwa; and if it is from al-naba’ meaning the news because he gives news about God, Exalted is He, then the hamza is changed to a wāw and then assimilated, like marwa.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the sixth chapter on al-sam‘iyyāt (the audible matters), which are the revealed truths. The author first states that this chapter contains sections, and then lists four major topics: prophethood, the resurrection, names and judgments (i.e., issues of faith and sin), and the imamate. He then begins the first chapter on prophethood. He gives a linguistic definition of nubuwwa: it means that a human being is sent from God to the creation. He then discusses the etymology of the word nabī. Nabī could be derived from nabāwah, meaning height or elevation, referring to the prophet's high status and fame; or from nabī meaning a path, because the prophet is a means to God; in that case, the word nubuwwa follows the pattern of ‘ubūwa. Alternatively, it could be derived from naba’, meaning a report, because the prophet reports from God; then the original hamza becomes wāw and is assimilated, as in the word marwa. Thus the definition is presented with linguistic analysis.

النبي
النباوة
النبأ

Checkpoint
What is the core meaning of nubuwwa according to the passage?
A) A human being receiving revelation
B) A human being sent from God to creation
C) An angelic messenger

Key Takeaway
The linguistic definition of nubuwwa shows that the prophet is a human sent by God, and the word may derive from elevation, path, or news.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the word 'prophet', which of these three meanings—elevated one, path, or news-bearer—resonates most with your current understanding? How does that shape your relationship with the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) today?
?
The passage says the prophet is 'a human being sent from the Truth to the creation'. Do you truly treat the Prophet (ﷺ) as a human being sent specifically to you, or is he just a distant historical figure? What would change if you fully believed he was sent for you personally?
?
The prophet as a 'means to the Truth' implies you need him to reach God. When was the last time you consciously used the Prophet's example as your path to Allah? Are you on that path right now, or have you wandered? What is one concrete step you can take today to follow that path?

Distinguishing Prophet and Messenger in Islamic Theology
Author defines nabi and rasul and discusses their distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of nabi and rasul, the key distinction that a messenger is typically given a new law and book, and the theological debate about whether God is obligated to send prophets.

Core Idea
A messenger is a prophet with a book or a new law; prophethood is a grace, not an obligation.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid lays the foundation for understanding prophethood by defining two key terms: nabi (prophet) and rasul (messenger). While both are humans sent by God to convey revelation, the author clarifies that a messenger is distinguished by having a specific law and scripture. He also addresses an objection from hadith and contrasts the Sunni view with the Mu‘tazila and philosophers regarding whether God must send prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the opening lines where the author defines nabi and rasul. Note the initial similarity and the specialization of rasul.
Pay attention to the phrase 'wa qad yukhaṣṣu...'
2
Understand the nature of prophetic sending
The author states that sending prophets is a grace (luṭf) and mercy, not obligatory upon God. Identify which groups oppose this view.
The Mu‘tazila and philosophers are mentioned.
3
Examine al-Jurjani's clarification
Read the marginal note by al-Sharif al-Jurjani on how revelation comes to a prophet versus a messenger.
Messenger receives specifically through Jibril with a book.
4
Analyze the hadith objection
Consider the objection from hadith that messengers exceed books. How does the author resolve it?
The response involves having a book or abrogating previous law.
5
Compare with Mu'tazila view
Read the final part where the Mu‘tazila define messenger and prophet differently. Note the difference in prophetic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: المبحث الأول. النبي إنسان بعثه الله لتبليغ ما أوحى اليه وكذا الرسول، وقد يخص بمن خص بشريعة وكتاب، والبعثة المتضمنها مصالح لا تحصى لطف من الله تعالى، ورحمة يختص بها من يشاء من عباده من غير وجوب عليه خلافاً للمعتزلة ولا عنه خلافاً للحكماء وبعض المتكلمين ذهاباً إلى ان مقتضى الحكمة يجب ان يقع لامتناع السفه كالمعلوم وقوعه لامتناع الجهل. (قال الشريف الجرجائي: النبي من أوحى إليه بملك أو ألهم في قلبه أو نبه بالرؤيا الصالحة فالرسول أفضل بالوحي الخاص الذي فوق وحي النبوة، لأن الرسول هو من أوحى إليه جبريل خاصة بتنزل الكتاب من الله تعالى.) الرسول الله له شريعة وكتاب فيكون أخص من النبي، واعترض بما ورد في الحديث من زيادة عدد الرسل على عدد الكتب، فقيل : هو من له كتاب أو نسخ لبعض أحكام الشريعة السابقة، والنبي قد يخلو عن ذلك كيوشع ( عليه السلام)، وفي كلام بعض المعتزلة أن الرسول صاحب الوحي بواسطة الملك والنبي هو المخبر عن تعالى بكتاب أو إلهام أو تنبيه في المنام.
His words: The first investigation. The prophet is a human being whom God sent to convey what was revealed to him, and likewise the messenger; and it may be specialized to the one who is specialized with a law and a book. And the sending, which includes benefits that cannot be counted, is a grace from God, Exalted is He, and a mercy that He bestows on whom He wills among His servants, without it being obligatory upon Him, contrary to the Mu‘tazila, nor from Him, contrary to the philosophers and some mutakallimūn who go to the view that the requirement of wisdom must occur, because futility is impossible, just as something known must occur because ignorance is impossible. (Al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī said: The prophet is the one to whom revelation is brought by an angel, or who is inspired in his heart, or who is alerted by a true dream; the messenger is more excellent by the special revelation that is above the revelation of prophethood, because the messenger is the one to whom Jibrīl specifically brings revelation with the descent of the Book from God, Exalted is He.) The messenger has a law and a book, so he is more specific than the prophet; and objection was raised by what occurred in the hadith about the number of messengers exceeding the number of books, so it was said: he is the one who has a book or who abrogates some rulings of the previous law; and the prophet may be free of that, like Yūsha‘ (peace be upon him). And in the speech of some Mu‘tazila: the messenger is the one who receives revelation through the mediation of an angel, while the prophet is the one who informs about God, Exalted is He, through a book, inspiration, or a dream prompting.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction between 'nabī' (prophet) and 'rasūl' (messenger). The author begins the first investigation (al-mabḥath al-awwal) by stating that both a prophet and a messenger are humans sent by God to convey what is revealed to them. However, the term 'rasūl' is often restricted to one who is given a specific law (sharī‘a) and a book. The author then adds that the act of sending (al-bi‘tha) contains countless benefits and is a grace (luṭf) and mercy from God, and is not obligatory upon God, contrary to the Mu‘tazila, nor does it come from necessary philosophical causes, contrary to the philosophers and some theologians who argue that wisdom dictates its necessity. The marginal note by al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī clarifies that a prophet may receive revelation through an angel, inspiration, or true dream, while a messenger is higher, being specifically visited by Jibrīl with a revealed book. The author then addresses an objection: a hadith seems to say there are more messengers than books, which contradicts the idea that every messenger has a book. The response is that a messenger either has a book or abrogates parts of a previous law; a prophet, like Yūsha‘, may have neither. The passage ends by citing the Mu‘tazila definition: a messenger receives revelation through an angel, while a prophet may receive it through a book, inspiration, or dream.

Key Terms
النبي
الرسول
الوحي
الشريعة
الكتاب
المعتزلة
الحكماء
اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All prophets are messengers and vice versa.
Correction: A messenger is a prophet with a book or new law; not all prophets are messengers.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distinguishing a messenger from a prophet?
A) A messenger has a book or abrogates previous law
B) A messenger performs miracles
C) A messenger is infallible
D) A messenger is never sent to a specific nation

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a divine grace, and while all messengers are prophets, a messenger is specifically distinguished by having a revealed law or scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that prophethood is a grace from God, or do you secretly feel that He owed you guidance? How would your worship change if you fully accepted that every prophet and every verse is a gift you did not deserve?
?
The messenger has a book and a law, while a prophet like Yusha‘ did not. You live after the final Book. Does that make you grateful, or do you take it for granted? What can you do today to show that you value this specific gift?
?
If God is not obligated to send prophets, then why did He send them? What does that tell you about His love and your responsibility? Are you living as if prophethood is a burden or a mercy?

Prophethood as Grace: The Ash'arī View
Taftazani defines prophethood as grace and refutes Mu'tazilite obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophethood is a divine grace (luṭf) from Allah, not an obligation, and that it does not depend on the prophet's merit.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a pure gift, not a reward for merit.

In this passage, al-Taftazani establishes the Ash'arī doctrine of prophethood as a grace from Allah. He contrasts the common people and philosophers, then corrects the Mu'tazilite notion of obligation. The key is that Allah sends prophets as a mercy, not because He is bound to.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial contrast
Notice how al-Taftazani sets the stage by showing that both common people and philosophers go astray without guidance.
2
Understand the definition
Focus on the sentence: 'فالحق أن البعثة لطف من الله تعالى ورحمة' – this is the core definition.
3
Note the refutation of Mu'tazila
The text rejects the idea that leaving it is evil; rather, it is merely good, not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طريق الحق وسبيل النجاة والرشاد مع اشتغالهم باكتساب أسباب المعاش، وخلو أكثرهم عن صناعة النظر وحذاقة الذهن، وعلى الفلاسفة المتشبثين بأذيال العقل العدول عن الصواب والوقوع في الضلال مع رجاحة عقولهم، ودقة أنظارهم وإقبالهم بالكلية على البحث عن المعارف الإلهية والعلوم اليقينية، وأنت خبير بأن في ترويج أمثال هذا المقال توسيع مجال الاعتزال فإنهم لا يعنون بالوجوب على الله تعالى سوى أن تركه لقبحه مخل بالحكمة ومظنة لاستحقاق المذمة، فالحق أن البعثة لطف من الله تعالى ورحمة يحسن فعلها ولا يقبح تركها على ما هو المذهب في سائر الألطاف، ولا تبتنى على استحقاق من المبعوث واجتماع أسباب وشروط فيه، بل الله تعالى يختص برحمته من يشاء من عباده وهو أعلم حيث يجعل رسالته . قال : وللمنكرين . شبه إحداها : أنها تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إليه ، ورد بجواز نصب الأدلة، أو خلق العلم الضروري.. الثاني: أنها عبث، لأن ما حسن عقلاً يفعل، وما قبح يترك، وما لم يحسن ولا يقبح يفعل حسب المصلحة، ورد بأنها تعاضد العقل فيما يستقل وتعاونه فيما لا يستقل الاحتمال فيما يظن، وتكون الطريق فيما لا يدرك مع أن التفويض إلى العقول وتدفع المتفاوتة مظنة اختلال النظام. الثالث: أن مبناها على التكليف بما لا ينتفع به العبد لتضرره، ولا المعبود لتعاليه مع ما فيه من شغل السر عن التوجه التام، ورد بأن نفعه جداً غالب . الرابع : ان في الشرائع ما يشعر بأنها ليست من عند الله كأفعال الصلاة والحج، والوضوء والغسل، وغير ذلك من الأمور الخارجة عن قانون العقل، ورد بأنها ابتلاء وتأكيد لملكة الامتثال عند الظاهريين، وحكم وأسرار خفية ظاهرة على المحققين . الخامس : القدح في المعجزة، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى).
The path of truth and the way of salvation and guidance, while they are occupied with acquiring means of livelihood, and most of them lack the craft of theoretical investigation and sharpness of intellect. And the philosophers clinging to the coattails of the intellect deviate from correctness and fall into misguidance despite the soundness of their intellects, the precision of their investigations, and their complete devotion to research on divine sciences and certain knowledge. And you are aware that promoting such articles expands the field of the Mu'tazila, for they do not mean by obligation upon Allah Almighty except that leaving it due to its ugliness is a defect in wisdom and a ground for deserving blame. So the truth is that the prophetic mission is a grace (luṭf) from Allah Almighty and a mercy; performing it is good and leaving it is not evil, according to the doctrine in all graces. And it is not based on any merit in the one sent or the gathering of causes and conditions in him. Rather, Allah Almighty singles out with His mercy whom He wills of His servants, and He knows best where to place His message. He said: And for the deniers. Their first doubt: that it depends on the sent one knowing that the sender is Allah Almighty, and there is no way to that. It is refuted by the permissibility of setting up proofs or creating necessary knowledge. The second: that it is futile, because what is rationally good is done, what is evil is avoided, and what is neither good nor evil is done according to interest. It is refuted by that it aids reason in what it is independent and helps it in what it is not independent of probability in what is conjectured, and becomes the way in what is not perceived, while delegating to varied intellects is a ground for disturbance of order. The third: that it is based on obligating what does not benefit the servant due to his harm, nor the worshipped due to His transcendence, along with what it contains of preoccupying the conscience from complete attention. It is refuted by that its benefit is overwhelmingly dominant. The fourth: that in the laws there is what suggests that they are not from Allah, such as acts of prayer, pilgrimage, ablution, washing, and other matters outside the law of reason. It is refuted by that they are a trial and a reinforcement of the faculty of compliance for the apparentists, and there are hidden wisdoms and secrets apparent to the investigators. The fifth: the objection to miracles, and it will come if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of prophethood and refutes five objections against its possibility. The author begins by contrasting the common people, who are preoccupied with livelihood and lack reasoning skills, with philosophers who cling to reason yet still go astray. He warns that promoting such doubts expands the Mu'tazilite position, which misconstrues obligation on God as requiring avoidance of ugliness. The correct view is that prophethood is a grace (luṭf) and mercy from God; performing it is good but leaving it is not evil, consistent with the Ashʿarī doctrine of graces (al-alṭāf). It does not depend on any merit in the prophet or conditions. Then he lists five objections from deniers with brief refutations. 1) Objection: the prophet must know the sender is God, but there is no way to that. Refutation: God can set up proofs or create necessary knowledge. 2) Objection: prophethood is futile because reason alone suffices; what is good is done, evil avoided, neutral acts follow interest. Refutation: prophethood aids reason in what it is independent and helps in what it is not; it guides in matters beyond reason, and delegating to varied intellects leads to disorder. 3) Objection: it burdens without benefit to servant or God. Refutation: its benefit is overwhelmingly dominant. 4) Objection: some religious acts (prayer, pilgrimage, ablution) seem irrational, suggesting not from God. Refutation: these are trials and reinforce compliance for the apparentists, but have hidden wisdoms known to the investigators. 5) Objection: miracles are suspect; deferred to later discussion.

Key Terms
لطف
البعثة
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood is based on the prophet's merit or qualifications.
Correction: Al-Taftazani states that prophethood is not based on any merit in the one sent or conditions; it is pure divine choice.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct view of prophethood?
A) It is obligatory upon God due to His wisdom.
B) It is a grace (lutf) from God; performing it is good but leaving it is not evil.
C) It is based on the merits of the prophet.
D) It is rationally necessary for human guidance.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a divine grace, not an obligation, and it is not based on the prophet's merit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does Taftazani's insistence that prophethood is not based on any merit or conditions in the recipient challenge your own sense of entitlement to Allah's guidance? Are there areas in your life where you feel you 'deserve' His mercy because of your deeds? What would it mean to truly live as if every blessing is a pure, undeserved grace?
?
If the sending of prophets is a grace that Allah was not obligated to give, then what is your personal responsibility toward the prophet (ﷺ) who was sent? In what concrete ways are you responding to that grace today? Does your daily life reflect gratitude for this gift, or do you treat it as a mere historical fact?
?
Consider Taftazani's contrast: common people lack theoretical investigation, philosophers deviate despite their intellect. Where do you see yourself in this spectrum? Are you relying on your own reasoning or busyness with worldly affairs? How does this passage call you to humbly seek the guidance that came through prophethood—not as a merit-earned reward but as a grace you must actively receive?

Five Objections to Prophethood and Their Refutations
Taftazani lists and refutes five objections to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five objections raised against the possibility of prophethood and how al-Taftazani refutes each.

Core Idea
Every objection to prophethood has a rational or revealed answer.

After establishing that prophethood is a grace, al-Taftazani addresses five objections from deniers. These objections challenge the epistemological basis, rationality, benefit, apparent irrationality of some commands, and the validity of miracles. Each is refuted concisely.

Guided Path
1
First objection: knowing the sender
Objection: How does the prophet know it's truly from God? Refutation: God can set up proofs or create necessary knowledge.
2
Second objection: futility
Objection: Reason suffices. Refutation: Reason needs aid, especially in matters beyond its grasp.
3
Third objection: no benefit
Objection: Obligation burdens without benefit. Refutation: Overwhelming benefit.
4
Fourth objection: irrational commands
Objection: Some acts seem irrational. Refutation: They are trials with hidden wisdoms.
5
Fifth objection: miracles
Objection: Miracles are questionable. Refutation: Deferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طريق الحق وسبيل النجاة والرشاد مع اشتغالهم باكتساب أسباب المعاش، وخلو أكثرهم عن صناعة النظر وحذاقة الذهن، وعلى الفلاسفة المتشبثين بأذيال العقل العدول عن الصواب والوقوع في الضلال مع رجاحة عقولهم، ودقة أنظارهم وإقبالهم بالكلية على البحث عن المعارف الإلهية والعلوم اليقينية، وأنت خبير بأن في ترويج أمثال هذا المقال توسيع مجال الاعتزال فإنهم لا يعنون بالوجوب على الله تعالى سوى أن تركه لقبحه مخل بالحكمة ومظنة لاستحقاق المذمة، فالحق أن البعثة لطف من الله تعالى ورحمة يحسن فعلها ولا يقبح تركها على ما هو المذهب في سائر الألطاف، ولا تبتنى على استحقاق من المبعوث واجتماع أسباب وشروط فيه، بل الله تعالى يختص برحمته من يشاء من عباده وهو أعلم حيث يجعل رسالته . قال : وللمنكرين . شبه إحداها : أنها تتوقف على علم المبعوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إليه ، ورد بجواز نصب الأدلة، أو خلق العلم الضروري.. الثاني: أنها عبث، لأن ما حسن عقلاً يفعل، وما قبح يترك، وما لم يحسن ولا يقبح يفعل حسب المصلحة، ورد بأنها تعاضد العقل فيما يستقل وتعاونه فيما لا يستقل الاحتمال فيما يظن، وتكون الطريق فيما لا يدرك مع أن التفويض إلى العقول وتدفع المتفاوتة مظنة اختلال النظام. الثالث: أن مبناها على التكليف بما لا ينتفع به العبد لتضرره، ولا المعبود لتعاليه مع ما فيه من شغل السر عن التوجه التام، ورد بأن نفعه جداً غالب . الرابع : ان في الشرائع ما يشعر بأنها ليست من عند الله كأفعال الصلاة والحج، والوضوء والغسل، وغير ذلك من الأمور الخارجة عن قانون العقل، ورد بأنها ابتلاء وتأكيد لملكة الامتثال عند الظاهريين، وحكم وأسرار خفية ظاهرة على المحققين . الخامس : القدح في المعجزة، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى).
The path of truth and the way of salvation and guidance, while they are occupied with acquiring means of livelihood, and most of them lack the craft of theoretical investigation and sharpness of intellect. And the philosophers clinging to the coattails of the intellect deviate from correctness and fall into misguidance despite the soundness of their intellects, the precision of their investigations, and their complete devotion to research on divine sciences and certain knowledge. And you are aware that promoting such articles expands the field of the Mu'tazila, for they do not mean by obligation upon Allah Almighty except that leaving it due to its ugliness is a defect in wisdom and a ground for deserving blame. So the truth is that the prophetic mission is a grace (luṭf) from Allah Almighty and a mercy; performing it is good and leaving it is not evil, according to the doctrine in all graces. And it is not based on any merit in the one sent or the gathering of causes and conditions in him. Rather, Allah Almighty singles out with His mercy whom He wills of His servants, and He knows best where to place His message. He said: And for the deniers. Their first doubt: that it depends on the sent one knowing that the sender is Allah Almighty, and there is no way to that. It is refuted by the permissibility of setting up proofs or creating necessary knowledge. The second: that it is futile, because what is rationally good is done, what is evil is avoided, and what is neither good nor evil is done according to interest. It is refuted by that it aids reason in what it is independent and helps it in what it is not independent of probability in what is conjectured, and becomes the way in what is not perceived, while delegating to varied intellects is a ground for disturbance of order. The third: that it is based on obligating what does not benefit the servant due to his harm, nor the worshipped due to His transcendence, along with what it contains of preoccupying the conscience from complete attention. It is refuted by that its benefit is overwhelmingly dominant. The fourth: that in the laws there is what suggests that they are not from Allah, such as acts of prayer, pilgrimage, ablution, washing, and other matters outside the law of reason. It is refuted by that they are a trial and a reinforcement of the faculty of compliance for the apparentists, and there are hidden wisdoms and secrets apparent to the investigators. The fifth: the objection to miracles, and it will come if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of prophethood and refutes five objections against its possibility. The author begins by contrasting the common people, who are preoccupied with livelihood and lack reasoning skills, with philosophers who cling to reason yet still go astray. He warns that promoting such doubts expands the Mu'tazilite position, which misconstrues obligation on God as requiring avoidance of ugliness. The correct view is that prophethood is a grace (luṭf) and mercy from God; performing it is good but leaving it is not evil, consistent with the Ashʿarī doctrine of graces (al-alṭāf). It does not depend on any merit in the prophet or conditions. Then he lists five objections from deniers with brief refutations. 1) Objection: the prophet must know the sender is God, but there is no way to that. Refutation: God can set up proofs or create necessary knowledge. 2) Objection: prophethood is futile because reason alone suffices; what is good is done, evil avoided, neutral acts follow interest. Refutation: prophethood aids reason in what it is independent and helps in what it is not; it guides in matters beyond reason, and delegating to varied intellects leads to disorder. 3) Objection: it burdens without benefit to servant or God. Refutation: its benefit is overwhelmingly dominant. 4) Objection: some religious acts (prayer, pilgrimage, ablution) seem irrational, suggesting not from God. Refutation: these are trials and reinforce compliance for the apparentists, but have hidden wisdoms known to the investigators. 5) Objection: miracles are suspect; deferred to later discussion.

العقل
المعجزة

D) By referring to miracles.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes five objections to prophethood by showing that each either is epistemologically or rationally answerable.

?

Definition of Revelation and Its Process
Defines revelation as connection of noble soul to unseen world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of revelation as the connection of a purified soul with the unseen world through the imaginative faculty, and how this results in audible divine speech.

Core Idea
Revelation is the waking vision of the unseen through a purified soul's imagination.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a detailed definition of revelation (al-wahy) that integrates philosophical and Sufi concepts. It describes the special qualities of the prophetic soul and the mechanism by which divine speech is received. As you read, notice how the author distinguishes revelation from mere internal experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's description of the soul and the revelation process sink in.
The passage is rich in technical terms from Islamic philosophy.
2
Identify the qualities of the prophetic soul
Look for the attributes that make a soul capable of receiving revelation: intense connection to the intellectual world, detachment from the sensory world, and a powerful imaginative faculty.
These qualities are prerequisites for the revelation event.
3
Understand the mechanism of revelation
Trace how the imaginative faculty receives forms from the Active Intellect and presents them as audible speech. Note that the source is explicitly God and angels, not the human soul.
This counters the misconception that revelation is purely internal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفس شريفة شديدة الاتصال بعالم العقل قليلة الالتفات إلى عالم الحس ومتخيلة شديدة جداً قوية التلقي من عالم الغيب، قليلة الانغماس في جانب الظاهر، لا يعصيها المصورة ولا يشغلها المحسوسات عن أفعالها الخاصة، ويحصل لذلك الإنسان في اليقظة أن يتصل بعالم الغيب، ويتمثل لقوته المتخيلة العقول المجردة والنفوس السماوية أشباحاً مصورة سيما العقل الفعال الذي له زيادة اختصاص بعالم العناصر، فتخاطبه وتحدث في سمعه كلاماً مسموعاً يحفظ ويتلي، ويكون ذلك من قبل الله وملائكته لا من الإنسان، وهذا معنى الوحي ونزول الملك والكتاب. وقد يكون ذلك على غاية الكمال، فيعبر عنها بمشاهدة وجه الله الكريم وسماع كلامه من غير واسطة.
A noble soul intensely connected to the world of intellect, little attentive to the world of sense, and with a very strong imagination capable of receiving from the world of the unseen, little immersed in the outward aspect; the faculty of representation does not disobey it, nor do sensible things occupy it from its own acts. For such a person, while awake, it happens that he connects to the world of the unseen, and his imaginative power is presented with the abstract intellects and celestial souls as formed phantoms, especially the Active Intellect, which has a special connection to the world of elements. They address him and produce in his hearing an audible speech that is memorized and recited. This is from God and His angels, not from the human. This is the meaning of revelation (al-waḥy) and the descent of the angel and the book. And this may be at its utmost perfection, so it is expressed as seeing the noble face of God and hearing His speech without an intermediary.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage provides a philosophical-sufi definition of revelation (al-waḥy). It describes the type of soul capable of receiving revelation: one that is intensely connected to the intellectual world and detached from the sensory world. The imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila) is particularly strong and receptive to the unseen realm. For such a person, in a waking state, the abstract intellects and celestial souls manifest as images before his imaginative power, especially the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) which has a special relation to the elemental world. These higher beings speak to him, producing audible speech that is preserved and recited. The author clarifies that this speech originates from God and His angels, not from the human soul itself. This is the meaning of revelation (waḥy) and the descent of the angel and scripture. At the highest level, this experience is described as beholding the noble face of God and hearing His speech directly without any intermediary.

عالم الحس
قوة متخيلة

وحي
مشاهدة وجه الله

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation is a purely internal psychological experience.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the speech during revelation comes from God and His angels, not from the human soul itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Revelation is a direct communication from God to a prophet through the mediation of the Active Intellect and the imaginative faculty, producing audible speech.

?

Defining I'jāz: Composition, Eloquence, and Beyond
Definition and classification of i'jāz by combining nazm and balāghah, plus two extra aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on what constitutes the miraculousness of the Quran, including the combination of composition and eloquence, and the additional aspects of prophetic reports.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle combines perfect composition and eloquence, plus knowledge of the unseen.

In this passage, the author classifies the theories of Quranic miraculousness into three main schools, then adds two more dimensions. The debate centers on whether the miracle lies in the wording, the meaning, or both. The author argues for the combination view and points to two additional miraculous aspects that transcend human capability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
Notice the two opposing schools and the third combining both. The passage attributes the third to al-Qadi.
2
Analyze the author's reasoning
See why the author argues that neither nazm nor balāghah alone suffices, using examples of human speech and Musaylimah.
The example of Musaylimah highlights a weak imitation.
3
Discover the additional aspects
The passage mentions two more aspects beyond nazm and balāghah: reports of past nations and future prophecies.
These are also part of i'jāz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليجعلا مذهبين متقابلين ، ويجعل كون الإعجاز بالأمرين جميعاً مذهباً ثالثاً ينسب إلى القاضي على ما قال إمام الحرمين أن وجه الإعجاز عندنا هو اجتماع الجزالة مع الأسلوب والنظم المخالف لأساليب كلام العرب من غير استقلال لاحدهما، إذ ربما يدعي أن بعض الخطب والأشعار من كلام أعاظم البلغاء لا ينحط عن جزالة القرآن انحطاطاً بيناً قاطعاً للأوهام، وربما يقدر نظم ركيك يضاهي نظم القرآن على ما روى من ترهات مسيلمة الكذاب الفيل وما أدريك ما الفيل له ذنب وثيل وخرطوم طويل. فلزم كون الإعجاز بالنظم البديع مع الجزالة، أعني البلاغة، وهو التعبير عن معنى سديد بلفظ شريف، وأن ينبيء عن المقصود من غير مزيد.
ثم قال: وفي القرآن سوى النظم والبلاغة وجهان آخران من الإعجاز الإخبار عن قصص الأولين من غير سماع وتلقين والإخبار عن المغيبات المستقبلة متكررة متوالية.
He makes two opposing schools, and makes the miraculousness by both matters together a third school attributed to al-Qadi, according to what Imam al-Haramayn said: that the aspect of miraculousness with us is the combination of majestic style (al-jazālah) with the style and composition (al-nazm) contrary to the styles of Arab speech, without either one being independent. For it may be claimed that some sermons and poems from the speech of the greatest rhetoricians do not descend from the majestic style of the Qur'an in a clear descent that cuts off doubts, and perhaps a weak composition can be estimated that resembles the composition of the Qur'an, as was narrated from the nonsense of Musaylimah the Liar: 'The elephant, and what will make you know what the elephant is? It has a tail, a testicle, and a long trunk.' So it is necessary that miraculousness is by the novel composition (al-nazm al-badīʿ) together with majestic style, meaning eloquence (al-balāghah), and it is the expression of a sound meaning with a noble word, and that it conveys the intended without excess.
Then he said: In the Qur'an besides composition and eloquence there are two other aspects of miraculousness: informing about the stories of the ancients without hearing or learning, and informing about future unseen matters repeatedly and consecutively.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the miraculousness (i'jāz) of the Qur'an and classifies its types. It begins by stating that there are two opposing schools: one that attributes i'jāz solely to the composition (nazm) and another that attributes it solely to the majestic style (jazālah) or eloquence (balāghah). A third school, attributed to al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), combines both, as stated by Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). The author then explains why the combination is necessary: it is possible that some human speeches match the Qur'an in majestic style but fall short in composition, or vice versa (as with Musaylimah's weak imitation). Therefore, the true i'jāz is the novel composition with majestic style, defined as expressing a sound meaning with a noble word without excess. Then he adds two more aspects of i'jāz beyond nazm and balāghah: reports of past nations (which Muhammad could not have known) and repeated prophecies of future events.

Key Terms
إعجاز
نظم
بلاغة
جزالة
مذهب
القاضي
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think i'jāz is solely in the wording (nazm) or solely in the meaning (balāghah).
Correction: The correct view combines both and also includes knowledge of the unseen.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many schools of thought regarding the nature of i'jāz are mentioned?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miraculousness consists of a unique combination of composition and eloquence, along with its knowledge of the past and future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite the Quran regularly—but do you approach it as a mere literary masterpiece, or as a living proof that demands your full submission? How would your relationship change if you saw every verse as both a miracle and a warning about your own unseen future?

?

Definition of the Saint and His Miracle
Definition of wali and karamah with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a saint (wali) and the nature of his miracle (karamah), including examples from Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
A saint's miracle differs from a prophet's miracle in that it is not accompanied by a claim to prophethood.

Before discussing the objections raised by the Mu'tazila, Sanusi establishes the foundational definitions. He defines the saint as a knower of Allah who turns away from everything else, and the karamah as a supernatural event that occurs through such a person without claiming prophethood. This lesson presents that definition along with well-known examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the precise meaning.
Notice the compact style of Sanusi's definitions.
2
Identify key elements of the definitions
Find the essential characteristics of wali and karamah as stated in the text.
Pay attention to the phrase 'bila da'wa al-nubuwwa'.
3
Review the examples
Consider the examples given (Maryam, Asif, Companions of the Cave) and note why they are considered karamat.
These are well-known cases in Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثامن - (الولي (۱) هو العارف بالله تعالى الصارف همته عما سواه. والكرامة ظهور أمر خارق للعادة من قبله بلا دعوى النبوة وهي جائزة ولو بقصد الولي ومن جنس المعجزات لشمول قدرة الله تعالى. وواقعة كقصة مريم (۲) ،وآصف، وأصحاب الكهف، وما تواتر جنسه من الصحابة والتابعين، وكثير من الصالحين وخالفت المعتزلة لأنها توجب التباس النبي بغيره، إذ الفارق هو المعجزة، والخروج عن بعض العادة لكثرة الأولياء، وانسداد باب إثبات النبوة لاحتمال أن تكون المعجزة ،إكراماً، لا تصديقاً، والإخلال بعظم قدر الأنبياء لمشاركة الأولياء. والجواب أن الكرامة لا تقارن دعوى النبوة، وكثرتها تكون استمرار نقض العادة، والمقارنة للدعوى تفيد القطع بالصدق عادة والكرامة تزيد جلالة قدر الأنبياء، حيث نالت أمتهم ذلك ببركة الاقتداء

(1) الولي :فعيل بمعنى الفاعل : وهو من توالت طاعته من غير أن يتخللها - صيان، أو بمعنى المفعول فهو من يتوالى عليه إحسان الله ،وأفضاله والولي : هو العارف بالله وصفاته بحسب ما يمكن المواظب على الطاعات المجتنب عن المعاصي المعرض عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات .
(2) قال تعالى : (كلما دخل عليها زكريا المحراب وجد عندها رزقاً قال يا مريم أنى لك هذا قالت: هو من عند الله إن الله يرزق من يشاء بغير حساب . سورة آل عمران آية رقم ٣٧ .
He said: The Eighth Investigation - The saint (1) is the knower of Allah, the Exalted, who diverts his concern from other than Him. And the miracle (karamah) is the appearance of a matter breaking the norm from him without claiming prophethood, and it is permissible even if intended by the saint, and it is of the same kind as miracles (mu'jizat) because of the universality of Allah's power. Instances include the story of Maryam (2), Asif, the Companions of the Cave, and what has been widely transmitted of its kind from the Companions, Successors, and many righteous people. The Mu'tazila opposed it because it would cause confusion between a prophet and others, since the distinguishing factor is the miracle, and violation of norms would become frequent due to the many saints, and the door of proving prophethood would be closed due to the possibility that the miracle is an honor, not a confirmation, and it would diminish the great status of prophets by sharing with saints. The answer: the karamah is not accompanied by a claim to prophethood; its frequency continues the breaking of the norm; accompaniment with the claim usually yields certainty of truthfulness; and the karamah increases the majesty of the prophets since their nation attains that by the blessing of following them.

(1) Al-wali is fa'il in the sense of the agent: it is one whose obedience is consecutive without being interrupted by [؟] sin, or in the sense of the passive: it is one upon whom Allah's beneficence and favors follow. And al-wali is the knower of Allah and His attributes as far as possible, the one who perseveres in acts of obedience, avoids sins, and turns away from indulgence in pleasures and desires.
(2) He said, Exalted: 'Whenever Zechariah entered the sanctuary, he found her with sustenance. He said, "O Mary, from where is this?" She said, "It is from Allah. Indeed, Allah provides to whom He wills without account."' Surah Al-Imran, verse 37.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the saint (wali) as a knower of Allah who turns his concern from everything else. The karamah is a supernatural event that occurs through him without a claim to prophethood. It is permissible and can be similar to a prophet's miracle because Allah's power encompasses all. Examples are given: Maryam receiving sustenance, Asif bringing the Queen of Sheba's throne, the Companions of the Cave. The Mu'tazila object on four grounds: (1) it blurs the distinction between prophet and saint, (2) violation of norms would become too frequent, (3) it would block proof of prophethood, (4) it diminishes the status of prophets. The answer: karamah is never accompanied by a claim to prophethood; its frequency does not harm because the accompanying claim to prophethood provides certainty; and karamah actually exalts the prophets because their nation benefits from their guidance. The footnote provides a detailed linguistic definition of al-wali and a Quranic verse for Maryam's story.

Key Terms
الولي
الكرامة
خارق للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamah is identical to a prophet's miracle.
Correction: The text specifies that karamah occurs 'without claiming prophethood', which distinguishes it from a prophetic miracle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a karamah?
A) A supernatural event accompanied by a claim to prophethood
B) A supernatural event without a claim to prophethood
C) A natural event
D) An event that only prophets experience

Key Takeaway
A saint's miracle (karamah) is a supernatural event that occurs through a righteous person without claiming prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that a saint is one who diverts his concern from everything other than Allah. If you examined your own concerns today, how much of your mental energy is spent on Allah versus on worldly distractions? What is the most persistent distraction you need to turn away from, and what step will you take tonight to begin that detachment?
?
The lesson teaches that a saint's miracle (karamah) is a supernatural event from Allah, without any claim to prophethood. Have you ever assumed that miracles require a prophet? How does this lesson change your understanding of Allah's ongoing interaction with His righteous servants? In what area of your life are you demanding a miracle rather than trusting His hidden favors?
?
Sanusi notes that karamat are permissible even if the saint intends them. This implies that the saint's intention does not diminish the miracle's divine origin. Reflect: when something good happens in your life, do you attribute it to your own planning or to Allah's favor? Write down one success you had this week and sincerely thank Allah for it, recognizing that He is the true source.

Refutation of Mu'tazila Objections to Karamah
Mu'tazila objections to karamah and Sanusi's answers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the concept of karamah and Sanusi's responses to each.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's objections to karamah are answered by the fact that karamah is never accompanied by a claim to prophethood.

Having defined the saint and his miracle, Sanusi now addresses the Mu'tazila who deny the possibility of karamah. They argue that it would blur the distinction between prophets and saints, make miracles too common, block proof of prophethood, and diminish the status of prophets. Sanusi systematically refutes each point, showing that the claim to prophethood is the decisive factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objections carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the four objections. Then read the translation.
The objections are given in a compact list.
2
Identify each of the four arguments
Isolate each objection: confusion, frequency, blocking proof, diminishing status.
Write them down in your own words.
3
Read Sanusi's responses
For each objection, note the corresponding answer in the text.
Notice how each answer hinges on the claim to prophethood.
4
Understand the logic
Reflect on why these answers resolve the objections. The key point is that prophets claim prophethood, saints do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثامن - (الولي (۱) هو العارف بالله تعالى الصارف همته عما سواه. والكرامة ظهور أمر خارق للعادة من قبله بلا دعوى النبوة وهي جائزة ولو بقصد الولي ومن جنس المعجزات لشمول قدرة الله تعالى. وواقعة كقصة مريم (۲) ،وآصف، وأصحاب الكهف، وما تواتر جنسه من الصحابة والتابعين، وكثير من الصالحين وخالفت المعتزلة لأنها توجب التباس النبي بغيره، إذ الفارق هو المعجزة، والخروج عن بعض العادة لكثرة الأولياء، وانسداد باب إثبات النبوة لاحتمال أن تكون المعجزة ،إكراماً، لا تصديقاً، والإخلال بعظم قدر الأنبياء لمشاركة الأولياء. والجواب أن الكرامة لا تقارن دعوى النبوة، وكثرتها تكون استمرار نقض العادة، والمقارنة للدعوى تفيد القطع بالصدق عادة والكرامة تزيد جلالة قدر الأنبياء، حيث نالت أمتهم ذلك ببركة الاقتداء

(1) الولي :فعيل بمعنى الفاعل : وهو من توالت طاعته من غير أن يتخللها - صيان، أو بمعنى المفعول فهو من يتوالى عليه إحسان الله ،وأفضاله والولي : هو العارف بالله وصفاته بحسب ما يمكن المواظب على الطاعات المجتنب عن المعاصي المعرض عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات .
(2) قال تعالى : (كلما دخل عليها زكريا المحراب وجد عندها رزقاً قال يا مريم أنى لك هذا قالت: هو من عند الله إن الله يرزق من يشاء بغير حساب . سورة آل عمران آية رقم ٣٧ .
He said: The Eighth Investigation - The saint (1) is the knower of Allah, the Exalted, who diverts his concern from other than Him. And the miracle (karamah) is the appearance of a matter breaking the norm from him without claiming prophethood, and it is permissible even if intended by the saint, and it is of the same kind as miracles (mu'jizat) because of the universality of Allah's power. Instances include the story of Maryam (2), Asif, the Companions of the Cave, and what has been widely transmitted of its kind from the Companions, Successors, and many righteous people. The Mu'tazila opposed it because it would cause confusion between a prophet and others, since the distinguishing factor is the miracle, and violation of norms would become frequent due to the many saints, and the door of proving prophethood would be closed due to the possibility that the miracle is an honor, not a confirmation, and it would diminish the great status of prophets by sharing with saints. The answer: the karamah is not accompanied by a claim to prophethood; its frequency continues the breaking of the norm; accompaniment with the claim usually yields certainty of truthfulness; and the karamah increases the majesty of the prophets since their nation attains that by the blessing of following them.

(1) Al-wali is fa'il in the sense of the agent: it is one whose obedience is consecutive without being interrupted by [؟] sin, or in the sense of the passive: it is one upon whom Allah's beneficence and favors follow. And al-wali is the knower of Allah and His attributes as far as possible, the one who perseveres in acts of obedience, avoids sins, and turns away from indulgence in pleasures and desires.
(2) He said, Exalted: 'Whenever Zechariah entered the sanctuary, he found her with sustenance. He said, "O Mary, from where is this?" She said, "It is from Allah. Indeed, Allah provides to whom He wills without account."' Surah Al-Imran, verse 37.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the saint (wali) as a knower of Allah who turns his concern from everything else. The karamah is a supernatural event that occurs through him without a claim to prophethood. It is permissible and can be similar to a prophet's miracle because Allah's power encompasses all. Examples are given: Maryam receiving sustenance, Asif bringing the Queen of Sheba's throne, the Companions of the Cave. The Mu'tazila object on four grounds: (1) it blurs the distinction between prophet and saint, (2) violation of norms would become too frequent, (3) it would block proof of prophethood, (4) it diminishes the status of prophets. The answer: karamah is never accompanied by a claim to prophethood; its frequency does not harm because the accompanying claim to prophethood provides certainty; and karamah actually exalts the prophets because their nation benefits from their guidance. The footnote provides a detailed linguistic definition of al-wali and a Quranic verse for Maryam's story.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
دعوى النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All miracles are the same whether from prophet or saint.
Correction: The text clarifies that the claim to prophethood accompanying a prophet's miracle provides certainty, while a saint's karamah lacks that claim.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what prevents karamah from causing confusion between saints and prophets?
A) Saints cannot perform miracles
B) Karamah is not accompanied by a claim to prophethood
C) Prophets only perform miracles
D) Karamah is less powerful

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's objections to karamah are refuted by the fact that karamah never accompanies a claim to prophethood, and that prophets' miracles are confirmed by their claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself seeking spectacular signs from God to confirm your faith, rather than being satisfied with the clear proof of prophethood? How does Sanusi's argument that karamah never accompanies a claim to prophethood challenge you to re-evaluate your sources of spiritual certainty?
?
When you hear about extraordinary spiritual experiences or blessings among believers today, do you sometimes feel envy or doubt? Reflect on the fact that these karamat actually increase the majesty of the Prophet ﷺ because they come from following him. How does this truth change how you view blessings in the Ummah and your own connection to the Prophet?
?
The Mu'tazila worried that karamah would lower the status of prophets. Have you ever unconsciously thought that God's favors to others somehow diminish His favors to you? How does Sanusi's answer — that karamah exalts the prophets — push you to humbly appreciate the blessings of being part of this nation, without feeling that they detract from prophethood?

Definition and Rational Basis of Sorcery
Author defines sorcery and states its rational and scriptural basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of sorcery and how it is both rationally permissible and established by scripture.

Core Idea
Sorcery is a real, teachable science, not mere illusion.

Sorcery is often misunderstood as mere trickery or magic. In this passage, the author grounds sorcery in a clear definition that distinguishes it from miracles and establishes its rational possibility and scriptural basis. Let's begin with the foundational definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Notice the key components: manifestation of a miraculous matter, specific actions, teaching/learning, evil of soul, opposition possible.
2
Identify the rational judgment
The author states that sorcery is rationally permissible (ja'iz aqlan). Ask yourself why something that seems supernatural can be considered rationally possible.
Compare to miracles and karamahs.
3
Scriptural proof
The Quranic verse 'They teach people sorcery' is cited. Reflect on how scripture affirms the existence and teaching of sorcery.
This is a sam'iyyat proof.
4
Distinguish from miracles
The expanded definition at the end highlights differences: sorcery is learned, not requested, specific to conditions. Note these contrasts.
This solidifies the unique nature of sorcery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث التاسع - السحر إظهار أمر خارق للعادة بمباشرة أعمال مخصوصة، يجري فيها التعليم والتعلم، وتعين عليها شرة النفس وتتأتى فيها المعارضة، وهو جائز عقلاً كالكرامة والمعجزة، وثابت سمعاً بقوله تعالى : ( يعلمون الناس السحر ) الآية . ولما ثبت من أنه سحر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وعائشة وابن عمر رضي الله عنهما، والطعن الكاذب من الكفرة في النبي بأنه مسحور أريد به زوال العقل بالسحر، والعصمة المشار إليها بقوله تعالى : والله يعصمك من الناس العصمة أن يهلكوه، أو يوقعوا خللاً في نبوته . وليس للساحر أن يفعل ما يشاء من الإضرار بالأنبياء، وإزالة ملك الخلفاء وغير ذلك . وقوله تعالى : ( يخيل إليه من سحرهم ) لا يدل على أن كل سحر تخييل وتمويه، بمنزلة الشعوذة على ما هو رأي المعتزلة . وأما الإصابة بالعين فتكاد تجري مجرى المشاهدات، وفيها نزل قوله تعالى : ( وإن يكاد الذين كفروا ليزلقونك بأبصارهم ) . واختلف القائلون بالسحر والعين في جواز الاستعانة بالرقى والعوذ، وفي جواز تعليق التمائم، والنفث، والمسح، والمسألة فرعية). إظهار أمر خارق للعادة من نفس شريرة خبيثة بمباشرة أعمال مخصوصة يجري فيها التعلم والتتلمذ، وبهذين الاعتبارين يفارق المعجزة والكرامة، وبأنه لا يكون بحسب اقتراح المقترحين، وبأنه يختص ببعض الأزمنة أو الأمكنة أو الشرائط،
He said: Ninth Investigation - Sorcery: The manifestation of a miracle-working matter through specific actions, in which teaching and learning occur, the evil of the soul assists it, and opposition is possible; it is rationally permissible like a karamah and a miracle, and established by revelation in His saying: 'They teach people sorcery' (Quran 2:102). And because it is established that the Prophet (peace be upon him), Aisha, and Ibn Umar (may God be pleased with them) were bewitched, and the false accusation of the disbelievers against the Prophet that he was bewitched intended the removal of reason by sorcery; and the protection indicated by His saying: 'And God will protect you from the people' (Quran 5:67) means protection from their killing him or causing a defect in his prophethood. And the sorcerer is not able to do whatever he wishes of harming the prophets, removing the rule of caliphs, and so on. And His saying: 'It appeared to him from their sorcery' (Quran 20:66) does not indicate that all sorcery is illusion and deception, like jugglery according to the Mu'tazilite view. As for affliction by the evil eye, it almost reaches the level of observed things, and regarding it was revealed His saying: 'And indeed those who disbelieve would almost make you slip with their eyes' (Quran 68:51). And those who affirm sorcery and the evil eye differed on the permissibility of seeking help with incantations and amulets, and on the permissibility of hanging talismans, blowing, wiping; the issue is secondary. The manifestation of a miracle-working matter from an evil, wicked soul through specific actions in which teaching and learning occur; in these two respects it differs from the miracle and the karamah, and in that it does not occur according to the request of the requesters, and in that it is specific to certain times, places, or conditions.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a definition and discussion of sorcery (sihr). It begins by introducing the ninth investigation: sorcery. The first definition: sorcery is the manifestation of a miracle-working matter through specific actions that can be taught and learned, aided by the evil of the soul, and can be opposed. It is rationally permissible like karamah (saintly miracle) and mu'jizah (prophetic miracle). It is established in revelation by the verse 'They teach people sorcery' (Quran 2:102). The author then mentions that sorcery was used against the Prophet, Aisha, and Ibn Umar, and the disbelievers falsely accused the Prophet of being bewitched, meaning loss of reason. The verse 'God will protect you' indicates preservation from death or damage to prophethood. A sorcerer cannot harm prophets or caliphs arbitrarily. Next, the author refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that all sorcery is mere illusion, citing that the verse 'it appeared to him from their sorcery' does not prove that. Then he discusses the evil eye ('ayn), stating it is practically observable and attested by Quran 68:51. Finally, there is a difference of opinion on using incantations, talismans, blowing, and wiping; it is a secondary matter. The passage ends with an expanded definition: sorcery is a miracle-working matter from an evil, wicked soul, distinguished from miracles and karamahs in two respects: it involves teaching/learning, and is not done at the request of others, and may be specific to times, places, or conditions.

Key Terms
السحر
المعجزة
الكرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sorcery is impossible or purely imaginary.
Correction: The author argues sorcery is rationally possible and affirmed by scripture, though it is not under the sorcerer's absolute control.

Checkpoint
What is the rational ruling on sorcery according to the author?
A) It is impossible
B) It is rationally permissible
C) It is obligatory
D) It is forbidden

Key Takeaway
Sorcery is a rationally possible phenomenon involving teachable actions, established by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage teaches that sorcery involves 'the evil of the soul' (شرّة النفس). When you face a difficult situation, do you ever feel the temptation to seek a shortcut that bends or breaks divine law? What 'evil of your soul' do you need to confront today?
?
The Quran states that people 'teach sorcery' (يعلمون الناس السحر). Are there areas of knowledge you pursue—formal or informal—that lead you away from reliance on Allah? How can you guard your learning so it draws you closer to Him, not into darkness?
?
If sorcery is a real, rationally permissible science that can be opposed, what does that mean for your view of the unseen world? Are you more aware of the reality of spiritual forces that oppose Allah's commands, and how does that awareness change your daily choices?

Sorcery and the Prophets: Protection and Refutation of Illusion
Author discusses sorcery's effect on prophets and refutes Mu'tazilite reduction of sorcery to illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sorcery can affect prophets externally but does not compromise their prophethood, and that it is not mere illusion as the Mu'tazilites claim.

Core Idea
Sorcery can afflict prophets but not undermine their mission.

A common objection to the reality of sorcery is that it cannot affect prophets. The author addresses specific historical instances where the Prophet and his companions were bewitched, and clarifies the limits of sorcery. He also refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that all sorcery is mere illusion. Let's examine these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the incidents
Read about the bewitchment of the Prophet, Aisha, and Ibn Umar. Understand that sorcery can affect even the best of creation.
This is based on authentic reports.
2
Divine protection
The verse 'God will protect you' is interpreted as protection from death or damage to prophethood, not from all harm.
This clarifies the scope of isma.
3
Limitation of sorcerer
Note the sentence: 'The sorcerer is not able to do whatever he wishes.' This refutes the idea of absolute power.
Sorcery has limits.
4
Refutation of Mu'tazila
The verse 'it appeared to him from their sorcery' is argued not to imply all sorcery is illusion. The author distinguishes between illusion (shu'badha) and real sorcery.
This is a key theological point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet himself was afflicted by sorcery, what makes you think you are immune from spiritual attacks? How are you actively guarding your faith against influences that seek to weaken it, rather than assuming you are simply protected?
?
The Mu'tazilites redefined sorcery as mere illusion to avoid a difficult implication for the Prophet's dignity. When have you changed or softened a truth of your faith because it made you uncomfortable? Will you commit to accepting difficult truths exactly as they come from the Revelation?
?
The Prophet was protected only from what would destroy his mission, not from all harm. In your life, have you been expecting God to shield you from every difficulty, when His promise is only to protect what matters most for your ultimate purpose? What suffering are you avoiding that might actually be part of His plan?

Evil Eye and Incantations; the Distinctive Nature of Sorcery
Author discusses the evil eye, incantations, and gives a final definition of sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reality of the evil eye as attested by revelation and experience, the differing opinions on incantations and talismans, and the final distinguishing features of sorcery.

Core Idea
The evil eye is nearly as observable as sorcery, and sorcery's true nature is evil and teachable.

After discussing sorcery's definition and effects, the author turns to the related phenomena of the evil eye and the use of incantations. He concludes with a refined definition that clearly separates sorcery from miracles. This lesson completes our understanding of these supranormal events.

Guided Path
1
Evidence for evil eye
Read the claim that the evil eye is almost matter of observation, and the Quranic verse that supports it.
The verse refers to disbelievers nearly causing the Prophet to slip with their eyes.
2
Difference on incantations
Note that scholars differed on using incantations, talismans, blowing, etc. This is a secondary (furu') issue.
3
Expanded definition of sorcery
The passage ends with an expanded definition: sorcery is from an evil, wicked soul, involves teaching/learning, is not at request, and is specific to conditions.
This distinguishes it from miracles and karamahs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث التاسع - السحر إظهار أمر خارق للعادة بمباشرة أعمال مخصوصة، يجري فيها التعليم والتعلم، وتعين عليها شرة النفس وتتأتى فيها المعارضة، وهو جائز عقلاً كالكرامة والمعجزة، وثابت سمعاً بقوله تعالى : ( يعلمون الناس السحر ) الآية . ولما ثبت من أنه سحر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وعائشة وابن عمر رضي الله عنهما، والطعن الكاذب من الكفرة في النبي بأنه مسحور أريد به زوال العقل بالسحر، والعصمة المشار إليها بقوله تعالى : والله يعصمك من الناس العصمة أن يهلكوه، أو يوقعوا خللاً في نبوته . وليس للساحر أن يفعل ما يشاء من الإضرار بالأنبياء، وإزالة ملك الخلفاء وغير ذلك . وقوله تعالى : ( يخيل إليه من سحرهم ) لا يدل على أن كل سحر تخييل وتمويه، بمنزلة الشعوذة على ما هو رأي المعتزلة . وأما الإصابة بالعين فتكاد تجري مجرى المشاهدات، وفيها نزل قوله تعالى : ( وإن يكاد الذين كفروا ليزلقونك بأبصارهم ) . واختلف القائلون بالسحر والعين في جواز الاستعانة بالرقى والعوذ، وفي جواز تعليق التمائم، والنفث، والمسح، والمسألة فرعية). إظهار أمر خارق للعادة من نفس شريرة خبيثة بمباشرة أعمال مخصوصة يجري فيها التعلم والتتلمذ، وبهذين الاعتبارين يفارق المعجزة والكرامة، وبأنه لا يكون بحسب اقتراح المقترحين، وبأنه يختص ببعض الأزمنة أو الأمكنة أو الشرائط،
He said: Ninth Investigation - Sorcery: The manifestation of a miracle-working matter through specific actions, in which teaching and learning occur, the evil of the soul assists it, and opposition is possible; it is rationally permissible like a karamah and a miracle, and established by revelation in His saying: 'They teach people sorcery' (Quran 2:102). And because it is established that the Prophet (peace be upon him), Aisha, and Ibn Umar (may God be pleased with them) were bewitched, and the false accusation of the disbelievers against the Prophet that he was bewitched intended the removal of reason by sorcery; and the protection indicated by His saying: 'And God will protect you from the people' (Quran 5:67) means protection from their killing him or causing a defect in his prophethood. And the sorcerer is not able to do whatever he wishes of harming the prophets, removing the rule of caliphs, and so on. And His saying: 'It appeared to him from their sorcery' (Quran 20:66) does not indicate that all sorcery is illusion and deception, like jugglery according to the Mu'tazilite view. As for affliction by the evil eye, it almost reaches the level of observed things, and regarding it was revealed His saying: 'And indeed those who disbelieve would almost make you slip with their eyes' (Quran 68:51). And those who affirm sorcery and the evil eye differed on the permissibility of seeking help with incantations and amulets, and on the permissibility of hanging talismans, blowing, wiping; the issue is secondary. The manifestation of a miracle-working matter from an evil, wicked soul through specific actions in which teaching and learning occur; in these two respects it differs from the miracle and the karamah, and in that it does not occur according to the request of the requesters, and in that it is specific to certain times, places, or conditions.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a definition and discussion of sorcery (sihr). It begins by introducing the ninth investigation: sorcery. The first definition: sorcery is the manifestation of a miracle-working matter through specific actions that can be taught and learned, aided by the evil of the soul, and can be opposed. It is rationally permissible like karamah (saintly miracle) and mu'jizah (prophetic miracle). It is established in revelation by the verse 'They teach people sorcery' (Quran 2:102). The author then mentions that sorcery was used against the Prophet, Aisha, and Ibn Umar, and the disbelievers falsely accused the Prophet of being bewitched, meaning loss of reason. The verse 'God will protect you' indicates preservation from death or damage to prophethood. A sorcerer cannot harm prophets or caliphs arbitrarily. Next, the author refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that all sorcery is mere illusion, citing that the verse 'it appeared to him from their sorcery' does not prove that. Then he discusses the evil eye ('ayn), stating it is practically observable and attested by Quran 68:51. Finally, there is a difference of opinion on using incantations, talismans, blowing, and wiping; it is a secondary matter. The passage ends with an expanded definition: sorcery is a miracle-working matter from an evil, wicked soul, distinguished from miracles and karamahs in two respects: it involves teaching/learning, and is not done at the request of others, and may be specific to times, places, or conditions.

Misconception: The evil eye is superstition with no basis.
Correction: The author affirms it is real and based on Quranic evidence.

Definition of Ma'ad and Two Types of Resurrection
Author defines ma'ad and contrasts bodily and spiritual resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ma'ad as bodily resurrection and the contrasting view of spiritual resurrection held by philosophers.

Core Idea
Islamic resurrection is bodily; philosophers conceive it as purely spiritual.

This lesson introduces the second chapter on resurrection (al-Ma'ad). The author first defines the term ma'ad and its literal meaning. He then clarifies that in the Islamic context, it refers to bodily resurrection, contrasting with the philosophers' concept of spiritual resurrection. By distinguishing the two, we see the core of Islamic eschatology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the definition of ma'ad and the two meanings presented.
2
Understand the literal meaning
The author explains that ma'ad is a verbal noun or place, and 'awd means returning to a previous state. This sets the groundwork.
3
Identify the two types of resurrection
Note the contrast between bodily resurrection (Islamic) and spiritual resurrection (philosophers). The first involves body and soul, the second only soul.
4
Reflect on the contrast
Ask yourself why the author emphasizes this difference. It highlights the Islamic commitment to bodily afterlife against philosophical abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثاني - في المعاد وفيه مباحث .وهو مصدر أو مكان، وحقيقة العود توجه الشيء إلى ما كان عليه، والمراد ههنا الرجوع إلى الوجود بعد الفناء، أو رجوع أجزاء البدن إلى الاجتماع بعد التفرق، وإلى الحياة بعد الموت، والأرواح إلى الأبدان بعد المفارقة، وأما المعاد الروحاني المحض على ما يراه الفلاسفة، فمعناه رجوع الأرواح إلى ما كانت عليه من التجرد عن علاقة البدن، واستعمال الآلات، أو التبرؤ عما ابتليت به من الظلمات.
He said: The second chapter: On Resurrection (al-Ma'ad), and it contains investigations. It (ma'ad) is a verbal noun or a place. The literal meaning of 'return' (al-'awd) is the turning of a thing back to what it was. What is meant here is returning to existence after annihilation, or the return of the parts of the body to assembly after separation, to life after death, and the souls to bodies after separation. As for purely spiritual resurrection according to the philosophers, its meaning is the return of souls to what they were before: being free from attachment to the body and the use of bodily instruments, or becoming free from the darknesses with which they were afflicted.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage introduces the second chapter on Resurrection (al-Ma'ad). The author explains that ma'ad can be a verbal noun meaning 'return' or a place of return. He defines the literal meaning of 'awd (return) as the turning of a thing back to its previous state. Then he specifies the intended meaning in this context: it refers to returning to existence after annihilation, which includes the reassembly of body parts, the restoration of life after death, and the reunion of souls with their bodies. This is the orthodox Islamic view of bodily resurrection. He then contrasts it with the philosophers' concept of purely spiritual resurrection (al-ma'ad al-rūḥānī al-mahḍ), which means the soul's return to a state of disembodiment and freedom from physical attachments and the darknesses of the material world. By distinguishing the two, the author sets the stage for discussing the Islamic doctrine of resurrection.

Key Terms
معاد
عود
بعث
روحاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quranic resurrection is only spiritual, like the philosophers' view.
Correction: The author clarifies that Islamic resurrection is bodily: the body is reassembled, life is restored, and souls reunite with bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary meaning of 'ma'ad' in this chapter?
A) A place of return.
B) The return of souls to their original disembodied state.
C) The return to existence after death, including bodily reunion.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Ma'ad in Islam means bodily resurrection after death, not mere spiritual return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your physical body — every part, every action, every sin — will be reassembled and held accountable? Or have you subconsciously adopted a vague hope of 'spiritual survival' that avoids the weight of bodily deeds? What would it mean for you to live today as if your body were eternally significant?
?
The philosophers saw the body as a darkness and burden to be shed. Do you sometimes view your body — its desires, its weaknesses — as an obstacle to your faith? How does the Islamic teaching that your body will be brought back to life challenge you to treat it as part of your worship, not just a temporary container?
?
If your resurrection is bodily, then every physical action you take — from how you eat to how you treat others — matters eternally. Are you living as if your body is destined for more than just dust? What one action will you change today to honor the body that Allah will raise on the Day of Judgment?
