Defining al-Ma'ad: The Return
Al-Taftazani defines al-Ma'ad and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Ma'ad as used in Islamic theology, including its linguistic meaning and the distinction between the bodily resurrection and the philosophers' spiritual return.

Core Idea
Al-Ma'ad is the return to existence after annihilation, encompassing both body and soul.

Before delving into the detailed investigations of the afterlife, the author clarifies the term 'al-Ma'ad' itself. This foundational definition sets the stage for the theological discussions that follow. It distinguishes between the Islamic understanding of a physical resurrection and the philosophical notion of a purely spiritual return.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and the literal English translation. Pay attention to the opening phrase 'The Second Chapter' which indicates a new topic.
Notice the linguistic explanation of al-ma'ad as a verbal noun or place.
2
Understand the linguistic meaning
The author explains that 'al-ma'ad' can mean a verbal noun or a place, and the essence of return (al-'awd) is the direction of a thing back to what it was. This sets the foundation.
3
Identify the intended meaning
The intended meaning in this chapter is the return to existence after annihilation, followed by specific elaborations: reunion of body parts, return to life, and reunion of souls with bodies.
This shows the comprehensive nature of the Islamic concept.
4
Note the philosophers' view
The author contrasts the Islamic view with the philosophers' purely spiritual return, which means souls return to an abstract state free from bodily attachments.
This distinction is important for later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثاني - في المعاد وفيه مباحث . وهو مصدر أو مكان، وحقيقة العود توجه الشيء إلى ما كان عليه، والمراد ههنا الرجوع إلى الوجود بعد الفناء، أو رجوع أجزاء البدن إلى الاجتماع بعد التفرق، وإلى الحياة بعد الموت، والأرواح إلى الأبدان بعد المفارقة، وأما المعاد الروحاني المحض على ما يراه الفلاسفة، فمعناه رجوع الأرواح إلى ما كانت عليه من التجرد عن علاقة البدن، واستعمال الآلات، أو التبرؤ عما ابتليت به من الظلمات.
He said: The Second Chapter – On al-Ma'ad (the Return), and it contains investigations. It (al-ma'ad) is a verbal noun or a place, and the reality of 'return' (al-'awd) is the direction of a thing to what it was upon. What is intended here is the return to existence after annihilation, or the return of the parts of the body to gathering after separation, and to life after death, and the souls to the bodies after separation. As for the purely spiritual return according to what the philosophers see, its meaning is the return of souls to what they were upon of abstraction from the attachment of the body and the use of instruments, or the disengagement from the darknesses with which they were afflicted.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-ma'ad' (the Return). It begins by stating that this is the second chapter and that it contains several investigations. It then gives the linguistic meaning: al-ma'ad can mean a verbal noun (masdar) or a place (makan), and the essence of 'return' (al-'awd) is the direction of a thing back to what it was. The intended meaning in this context is the return to existence after annihilation, the reunion of body parts, the return to life after death, and the reunion of souls with bodies after separation. It then mentions the philosophers' view of a purely spiritual return, which is the return of souls to a state of abstraction from bodily attachments and instruments, or a purification from the darknesses of the material world.

Correction: While the primary Islamic understanding includes physical resurrection, the term also accommodates a purely spiritual return as held by some philosophers, which the author distinguishes.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Qur'an and Sunnah emphasize bodily resurrection. Have you ever doubted the reality of the resurrection of the body? What makes you hold onto that doubt?

Not Abrogation But Explanation: Later Specification of General Texts
Objection and answer about later specification of general texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why later specification of a general text is considered explanation (bayān) rather than abrogation (naskh) in Islamic legal theory.

Core Idea
Later evidence is explanation, not abrogation — it reveals what was always intended.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection to the permissibility of later particularization of general texts. Critics argue that if a general statement is later narrowed, it would imply that the earlier statement was false — an impossibility in divine reports. The author responds by denying the premise and clarifying that such later specification is a common and valid form of explanation, not abrogation. This lesson unpacks the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that a general form without evidence of particularity implies all individuals. Later exclusion would be abrogation, invalid in reports.
Notice the claim that connected evidence is required for valid specification.
2
Identify the denial and proof
The author denies that specification requires connected evidence, citing common practice of intending specific from general without it.
The author uses 'mamnūʿ' (denied) to reject the premise.
3
Understand the conclusion
The later evidence is an explanation (bayān) of the original intent, not abrogation. This is the doctrine of Shafi'i and early Hanafi jurists.
Contrast with the objector's view: specification always abrogates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : صيغة العموم المتعرية عن دليل الخصوص تدل على إرادة كل فرد مما يتناوله اللفظ بمنزلة التنصيص عليه باسمه الخاص. فإخراج البعض بدليل متراخ يكون نسخاً، وهو لا يجري في الخبر للزوم الكذب. وإنما التخصيص هو الدلالة على أن المخصوص غير داخل في العموم ، ولا يكون ذلك إلا بدليل متصل . قلنا : ممنوع، بل إرادة الخصوص من العام والتقييد من المطلق شائع من غير دليل متصل . ثم دليل التخصيص والتقييد بعد ذلك ، وإن كان متراخياً بيان لا نسخ . وهذا هو المذهب عند الفقهاء الشافعية ، والقدماء من الحنفية . وكانوا ينسبون...
For if it is said: The general form (ṣīghat al-ʿumūm) that is bare of evidence of particularity indicates intending every individual that the word encompasses, like the specification (tanṣīṣ) upon it by its particular name. So the exclusion of some by a later evidence would be abrogation (naskh), and that is not applicable to reports (khabar) because it would necessitate lying. And particularization (takhṣīṣ) is only the indication that the particularized is not included in the general, and that only occurs by a connected evidence (dalīl muttaṣil). We say: It is denied (mamnūʿ); rather, intending the specific from the general and the qualification (taqyīd) of the absolute is common without a connected evidence. Then the evidence of particularization and qualification after that, even if it is later, is an explanation (bayān), not abrogation. And this is the doctrine of the Shafi'i jurists and the early Hanafis. And they used to attribute...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning the interpretation of general threats. The objector says that a general expression that is not accompanied by evidence of particularity indicates that every individual is intended, as if each were specifically named. Therefore, to exclude some by a later evidence would be abrogation (نسخ), which is invalid in reports because it would imply that God's earlier statement was false. The correct particularization (تخصيص) is only valid if the evidence is connected (متصل) to the general statement. The author responds by denying this claim: it is common practice in Islamic jurisprudence to intend specific from a general expression and to qualify an absolute statement without a connected evidence. The later evidence that narrows the scope is considered a clarification (بيان), not abrogation. This is the established doctrine of the Shafi'i and early Hanafi jurists.

Key Terms
العموم
الخصوص
التخصيص
النسخ
البيان
الدليل المتصل
الدليل المتراخي
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That later specification always implies abrogation (naskh) and thus is invalid in reports.
Correction: Later specification is not abrogation; it is explanation (bayān) of what was always intended, as common practice shows.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how is later evidence of particularization regarded?
A) As abrogation.
B) As explanation.
C) As lying.
D) As disconnected.

Key Takeaway
Later specification of general texts is a form of explanation (bayān) that clarifies the originally intended scope, not abrogation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of punishment and then a verse of mercy, do you ever feel that God has contradicted Himself? How does this lesson's distinction between abrogation and explanation challenge that feeling, and what does your reaction reveal about your trust in His consistency?
?
Have you ever used a general command from the Quran or hadith to justify being harsh toward someone, while ignoring later qualifications that show mercy? How will you change your approach to scripture so that you don't turn general texts into weapons?
?
If God's word is timeless and perfect, why do you feel uneasy when later revelations seem to narrow earlier promises? What does this lesson ask you to believe about God's clarity and purpose in revelation?

The Definition of Repentance as Regret for Sin
Sanusi defines repentance linguistically and in Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and Sharia definition of repentance as regret for sin motivated by its ugliness, not by other factors.

Core Idea
True repentance is regret for sin because it is a sin.

Sanusi begins the fourteenth topic by defining repentance. He clarifies that repentance is not merely abandoning a sin, but a specific kind of regret. This lesson unpacks that definition and distinguishes it from other forms of regret, such as regret for worldly consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that Sanusi starts with the topic and then gives the core definition: 'الندم على المعصية لكونها معصية'.
2
Understand the linguistic meaning
The author explains that 'tawba' linguistically means 'return'. When used for a servant, it means returning from sin to regret; for God, it means returning blessings. This sets the stage for the Sharia definition.
3
Identify the essential condition
The Sharia definition emphasizes that regret must be for the sin itself (لكونها معصية). The author contrasts this with regret due to bodily harm, loss of honor, or wealth, which do not count as repentance.
This is the key distinction.
4
Reflect on the sign of regret
The passage mentions that the sign of true regret is prolonged remorse and weeping. The Mu'tazila said it suffices to believe it is wrongdoing and to wish to undo it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المبحث الرابع عشر - في التوبة.
الندم على المعصية لكونها معصية. وهل الندم لخوف النار، أو طمع الجنة، ولقبح المعصية مع غرض آخر، وعند مرض مخوف توبة؟ فيه تردد، وقد يزاد قيد العزم على الترك في الاستقبال ويزاد على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار حتى لو سلب القدرة، لم يشترط العزم على الترك. والظاهر أنه للبيان دون الاحتراز ومعنى الندم الأسف والحزن، وتمنى كونه لم يفعل.
وعلامته طول الحسرة والبكاء. واكتفى المعتزلة باعتقاد أنه إساءة، وأنه لو أمكنه رد المعصية، لردها. لأن أهل الجنة يندمون على تقصيرهم، ولا حزن ولأن العاصي مكلف بالتوبة دائماً. وقد لا يمكنه تحصيل الحزن)
وهي في اللغة الرجوع . يقال : تاب وناب أو أناب إذا رجع . فإذا أسند إلى العبد، أريد رجوعه عن الزلة إلى الندم . وإذا أسند إلى الله تعالى، أريد رجوع نعمه وألطافه إلى عباده .
وفي الشرع هي الندم على المعصية لكونها معصية، وقيد بذلك، لأن الندم على المعصية لإضرارها ببدنه ، أو إخلالها بعرضه أو ماله أو نحو ذلك لا تكون توبة . وأما الندم لخوف النار، أو طمع الجنة، فهل تكون توبة؟ فيه تردد مبني على أن ذلك هل يكون ندماً عليها لقبحها ولكونها معصية أم لا؟ وكذا في الندم عليها لقبحها مع غرض آخر. والحق أن جهة القبح إن كانت بحيث لو انفردت لتحقق الندم فتوبة وإلا فلا كما إذا كان الغرض مجموع الأمرين، لا كل واحد منهما . وكذا في التوبة عند مرض مخوف بناء على أن ذلك الندم هل يكون لقبح المعصية أم لا؟ بل للخوف، كما في الآخرة عند معاينة النار، فيكون بمنزلة إيمان اليأس.
والظاهر من كلام النبي عليه السلام قبول التوبة ما لم تظهر علامات الموت. ومعنى الندم تحزن وتوجع على أن فعل وتمني كونه لم يفعل. ولا بد هذا للقطع بأن مجرد الترك كالماجن إذا مل مجونه فاستروح إلى بعض من المباحات ليس توبة . ولقوله ( عليه السلام) الندم توبة.
وقد يزاد قيد العزم على ترك المعاودة في المستقبل. واعترض بأن فعل المعصية في المستقبل قد لا
He said: The Fourteenth Topic - On Repentance.
Regret for disobedience because it is disobedience. And is regret due to fear of the Fire, or hope of Paradise, or due to the ugliness of the sin along with another purpose, and at a fearful illness, (considered) repentance? There is hesitation concerning it. And the condition of resolve to abandon (the sin) in the future is sometimes added, and it is added on the condition of occurrence and ability, so that if ability is removed, resolve to abandon is not required. And the apparent meaning is that (the condition) is for clarification, not for exclusion. And the meaning of regret is grief and sorrow, and wishing that one had not done it.
And its sign is prolonged remorse and weeping. The Mu'tazila sufficed with believing that it is wrongdoing, and that if he could undo the sin, he would undo it, because the people of Paradise will regret their shortcomings without grief, and because the sinner is obligated to repent always, and he may not be able to attain grief.
And it (tawba) linguistically means return. It is said: tāba, nāba, or anāba if he returned. When attributed to the servant, it means his return from the slip to regret. When attributed to God Almighty, it means the return of His blessings and favors to His servants.
And in the Sharia, it is regret for the sin because it is a sin. It is qualified by that because regret for the sin due to its harm to his body, or its damaging his honor, wealth, or the like, is not repentance. As for regret due to fear of the Fire or hope of Paradise, is it repentance? There is hesitation based on whether that is regret for it because of its ugliness and because it is a sin, or not. Similarly regarding regret for it due to its ugliness along with another purpose. The truth is that if the aspect of ugliness is such that if it were alone regret would be realized, then it is repentance; otherwise not, as when the purpose is the combination of the two, not each one alone. Similarly regarding repentance during a fearful illness, based on whether that regret is due to the ugliness of the sin or not, but rather due to fear, as in the Hereafter when seeing the Fire, so it is like the faith of despair.
And the apparent meaning from the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is the acceptance of repentance as long as the signs of death have not appeared. And the meaning of regret is to be grieved and pained that one did it, and to wish that one had not done it. This is necessary to be certain that mere abandonment, like a libertine who becomes bored with his licentiousness and then inclines toward some permissible things, is not repentance. And because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Regret is repentance.'
And sometimes the condition of resolve not to repeat in the future is added. And it is objected that committing the sin in the future may not...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a definition and detailed discussion of repentance (tawba). The author begins by stating that repentance is regret for the sin because it is a sin. He then raises the question whether regret motivated by fear of Hell, hope of Paradise, or other factors counts as repentance, noting that there is hesitation. Next he discusses the added condition of resolve not to return to the sin, but clarifies that this condition is for clarification rather than exclusion. The linguistic meaning of 'tawba' is 'return,' and when said of the servant it means returning from error to regret, and when said of God it means returning blessings. The Sharia definition emphasizes that regret must be for the sin itself, not for worldly consequences. He then treats the problematic cases of regret arising from fear, hope, or a combination of motives, concluding that if the ugliness of the sin alone would cause regret, it is valid repentance; otherwise not. The same applies to repentance during illness. He cites the prophetic hadith 'Regret is repentance' and notes the condition of resolve not to repeat, though some object that future sin is not within control.

الندم
المعصية
الرجوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any regret for a sin suffices for repentance, even if it is due to worldly harm or fear of punishment.
Correction: The author clarifies that genuine repentance requires regret specifically because the act is a sin, not because of its harmful consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the defining characteristic of valid repentance?
A) Regret for the sin due to its harmful consequences.
B) Regret for the sin because it is a sin (for its ugliness).
C) Regret combined with fear of Hell.
D) Abandonment of the sin without regret.

Key Takeaway
Repentance is defined as regret for the sin itself, not for any other reason, and its sign is prolonged remorse and wishing it had not been done.

What Is Kalam?

Linguistic and Technical Definition of Repentance
Defines tawba linguistically and technically, with conditions and borderline cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definition of repentance (tawba), the central role of genuine regret for the sin itself, and how to distinguish true repentance from regret motivated by worldly or other concerns.

Core Idea
True repentance is not just stopping sin, but heartfelt regret for the sin because it is a sin.

Repentance (tawba) is a central concept in Islamic theology. Before we can understand its implications, we must first define it properly. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid provides a precise linguistic and technical definition, emphasizing that the essence of repentance is regret (nadam) over the sin for its own ugliness. The author also addresses borderline cases to clarify what does and does not count as true repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's definitions and distinctions sink in before moving to commentary.
Notice the contrast between linguistic and technical definitions.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the author gives the linguistic meaning (return) and the technical meaning (regret for sin because it is a sin). Note how the restriction 'because it is a sin' is crucial.
This restriction excludes regret for worldly harm.
3
Examine the borderline cases
The author discusses regret for fear of Hell, hope of Paradise, mixed motives, and terminal illness. Ask yourself: under what conditions does regret count as repentance? The answer depends on whether the ugliness of the sin alone would have caused regret.
The 'faith of despair' (iman al-ya's) is compared to repentance at the sight of death.
4
Understand the essence of regret
The passage ends with the meaning of regret (sadness and wishing it hadn't happened) and the Prophetic saying 'Regret is repentance.' Consider why mere cessation of sin is insufficient without this heartfelt regret.
This distinguishes true repentance from mere behavior change.

وهي في اللغة الرجوع . يقال : تاب وناب أو أناب إذا رجع . فإذا أسند إلى العبد، أريد رجوعه عن الزلة إلى الندم . وإذا أسند إلى الله تعالى، أريد رجوع نعمه وألطافه إلى عباده . وفي الشرع هي الندم على المعصية لكونها معصية، وقيد بذلك، لأن الندم على المعصية لإضرارها ببدنه ، أو إخلالها بعرضه أو ماله أو نحو ذلك لا تكون توبة . وأما الندم لخوف النار، أو طمع الجنة، فهل تكون توبة؟ فيه تردد مبني على أن ذلك هل يكون ندماً عليها لقبحها ولكونها معصية أم لا؟ وكذا في الندم عليها لقبحها مع غرض آخر. والحق أن جهة القبح إن كانت بحيث لو انفردت لتحقق الندم فتوبة وإلا فلا كما إذا كان الغرض مجموع الأمرين، لا كل واحد منهما . وكذا في التوبة عند مرض مخوف بناء على أن ذلك الندم هل يكون لقبح المعصية أم لا؟ بل للخوف، كما في الآخرة عند معاينة النار، فيكون بمنزلة إيمان اليأس. والظاهر من كلام النبي عليه السلام قبول التوبة ما لم تظهر علامات الموت. ومعنى الندم تحزن وتوجع على أن فعل وتمني كونه لم يفعل. ولا بد هذا للقطع بأن مجرد الترك كالماجن إذا مل مجونه فاستروح إلى بعض من المباحات ليس توبة . ولقوله ( عليه السلام) الندم توبة
And it (tawba) in language is returning. It is said: تَابَ and نَابَ or أَنَابَ when he returns. So when attributed to the servant, what is meant is his return from the slip to regret. And when attributed to God Almighty, what is meant is the return of His blessings and favors to His servants. And in the Sacred Law it is regret over the sin because it is a sin, and it is restricted by that, because regret over the sin for its harm to his body, or its damage to his honor or wealth or the like, is not repentance. As for regret for fear of the Fire, or hope for Paradise, is it repentance? There is hesitation about it, based on whether that is regret over it for its ugliness and because it is a sin or not? Similarly, regarding regret over it for its ugliness together with another purpose. The truth is that if the aspect of ugliness is such that if it were alone, regret would be realized, then it is repentance; otherwise it is not, as when the purpose is both matters together, not each one individually. And similarly concerning repentance at a frightening illness, based on whether that regret is for the ugliness of the sin or not? Rather, it is for fear, as in the Hereafter at the sight of the Fire, so it is like the faith of despair. And it is apparent from the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that repentance is accepted as long as the signs of death have not appeared. And the meaning of regret is sadness and pain over what one did and wishing that one had not done it. And this is necessary to be certain that mere abandonment, like a debauched person who tires of his debauchery and then resorts to some permissible things, is not repentance. And because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Regret is repentance.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition of repentance (tawba). It begins with the linguistic meaning: tawba means 'return'. When used of a servant, it means returning from sin to regret; when used of God, it means His turning back with blessings. Then it gives the technical (Shari) definition: regret (nadam) over the sin because it is a sin. The author stresses the restriction 'because it is a sin' to exclude regret for worldly harm. He then examines borderline cases: regret for fear of hell or hope of paradise, or for ugliness mixed with other motives. The correct view is that if the ugliness alone would have produced regret, it counts as repentance; otherwise not. He extends this to repentance during terminal illness, cautioning that if the regret is merely from fear of death, it resembles the 'faith of despair' (iman al-ya's) and is not accepted. Finally, he cites the Prophet's teaching that regret itself is repentance, and explains regret as sorrow and the wish not to have done the act, distinguishing it from mere cessation of sin.

معصية
إيمان اليأس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think repentance is merely stopping the sin or abandoning the act without any internal regret.
Correction: The passage clarifies that true repentance requires genuine regret for the sin because it is a sin, not just ceasing the action or regretting its worldly consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct condition for regret over a sin to count as repentance?
A) Regret due to fear of punishment or hope of reward.
B) Regret due to harm caused to one's body or honor.
C) Regret due to the sin being ugly and a sin in itself, such that the ugliness alone would produce regret.
D) Regret combined with a resolve to never return to the sin.

Key Takeaway
The core of repentance is genuine regret over the sin because it is a sin, not merely abandoning it or regretting its consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you frequently commit. When you feel regret, is it because of its worldly consequences (e.g., embarrassment, lost time, health issues) or because it disobeys Allah? Be honest: if you were guaranteed no worldly harm and no punishment, would you still feel sorrow for it? What does your answer reveal about the sincerity of your repentance?
?
You have repented out of fear of Hellfire countless times—but have you ever checked whether your regret would exist if Hellfire were not a factor? If your repentance depends on fear rather than love and reverence for Allah, is it truly repentance? What will you do today to cultivate regret for the ugliness of sin itself?
?
Consider the sin you abandoned simply because you lost interest or grew tired of it—like a hobby you dropped. Was that repentance? If not, what is missing? Will you go back to that sin in your heart and feel genuine sorrow for offending your Creator, or will you let it remain as mere abandonment?

The Requirement of Resolve in Repentance for the Incapable
The author explains why resolve is not required for repentance when the sinner is incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why resolve (ʿazm) is not required for repentance when a person is incapable of repeating the sin, and how Imam al-Haramayn's statement clarifies this.

Core Idea
Resolve in repentance is for those who can, not for those who cannot.

This lesson addresses a subtle point in the theology of repentance. When someone has lost the ability to commit a sin, does their repentance still require a resolve to abandon it? The author presents the problem and provides an answer rooted in the estimation of ability, citing Imam al-Haramayn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the problem
Notice the scenarios where a sin occurs to the mind but cannot be acted upon. Why would resolve be inconceivable?
2
Identify the answer given
The answer distinguishes between actual and estimated capability. Understand the key phrase 'on the estimation of occurrence and capability'.
3
Examine Imam al-Haramayn's statement
His words show that resolve is not always required. Why is resolve invalid for a eunuch or mute?
This illustrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخطر بالبال لذهول أو جنون أو موت ونحو ذلك . وقد لا يقتدر عليه لعارض آفة كخرس في القذف وشلل أوجب في الزنا، فلا يتصور العزم على الترك لما فيه من الإشعار بالقدر والاختيار. فأجيب بأن المراد العزم على الترك على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار ، حتى لو سلب القدرة لم يشترط العزم على الترك. بهذا يشعر كلام إمام الحرمين حيث قال : إن العزم على ترك المعاودة إنما يقارن التوبة في بعض الأحوال، ولا يطرد في كل حال، إذا العزم إنما يصح فيمن يتمكن من مثل ما قدمه ولا يصح من المجبوب العزم على ترك الزنا، ولا من الأخرس العزم على ترك القذف فما ذكر في المواقف (۱) من أن قولنا : إذا قدر لأن من سلب القدرة على الزنا، وانقطع طمعه عن عود القوة إذا عزم على تركه ، لم يكن ذلك توبة منه ، ليس على ما ينبغي لإشعاره بأن العزم على الترك يصح مع عدم القدرة على الفعل وبأن الندم على الفعل مع العزم على الترك لا يكفي في التوبة، لكن لا بد من أمر ثالث هو بقاء القدرة وكلام الإمام وغيره أن عند عدم القدرة لا يشترط في التوبة العزم بل لا يصح ويكفي مجرد الندم . لا يقال : مراد المواقف أن مجرد هذا العزم بدون الندم ليس بتوبة، لأنا نقول : هذا لغو (۲) من الكلام، لا بيان لفائدة التقييد بالقدرة.
(۱) هو كتاب مكون من ثمانية أجزاء في أربعه مجلدات طبع قديما وعليه بعض الحواشي ويعتبر من أهم المراجع في علم الكلام للأشاعرة وصاحبه عضد الدين الايجي يسمى : عبد الرحمن بن أحمد توفي عام ٧٥٦هـ .
(۲) اللغو الباطل، وهو يشتمل الشرك كما قاله بعضهم، والمعاصي كما قاله آخرون، وما لا فائدة فيه من الأقوال والأفعال كما قال تعالى والذين هم عن اللغو معرضون . سورة المؤمنون آية رقم ٢٣ وقال تعالى : (وإذا مروا باللغو مروا كراماً .. سورة الفرقان آية رقم ٧٢
It occurs to the mind due to heedlessness, madness, death, or the like. And one may not be able to commit it due to a defect such as muteness in slander or paralysis that prevents in adultery, so the resolve to abandon is inconceivable because it implies power and choice. So it was answered: what is intended is the resolve to abandon on the estimation of occurrence and capability, so that even if power is removed, the resolve to abandon is not required. This is suggested by the words of Imam al-Haramayn where he said: Verily, the resolve to abandon repetition only accompanies repentance in some states, and is not consistent in every case, because resolve is only valid for one who is able to do something like what he did before, and it is not valid for the eunuch to resolve to abandon adultery, nor for the mute to resolve to abandon slander. So what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif that our saying 'if he is able' because one who is deprived of power over adultery and his hope of returning strength is cut off, if he resolves to abandon it, that is not repentance from him, is not as it should be, because it suggests that resolve to abandon is valid with inability to act, and that regret for the act with resolve to abandon is not sufficient in repentance, but rather a third thing is necessary, which is the continuation of power. And the words of Imam and others indicate that when there is no power, resolve is not required in repentance, rather it is not valid, and mere regret suffices. It should not be said: The meaning of al-Mawaqif is that mere resolve without regret is not repentance, for we say: This is nonsense speech, not an explanation of the benefit of restricting with power.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the role of 'azm (resolve) in tawba (repentance) for a person who has lost the ability to commit the sin (e.g., due to illness, disability, or death). The author begins by noting that a sin may occur to the mind but one may be unable to act due to an impediment. In such cases, requiring a resolve to abandon the sin would suggest that the person has power and choice, which they lack. The answer given is that the intended resolve is on the supposition of the sin occurring and of ability, so even if ability is removed, resolve is not required. The author cites Imam al-Haramayn, who states that resolve to abandon repetition accompanies repentance only in some cases, not always, because resolve is valid only for one who can actually repeat the sin; a eunuch cannot resolve to abandon adultery, nor a mute to abandon slander. The author then critiques a statement in al-Mawaqif which claims that if one deprived of power resolves to abandon, it is not repentance, arguing that this implies resolve is valid without ability and that regret plus resolve is insufficient without a third condition (continued ability). The correct view, according to Imam al-Haramayn, is that when ability is absent, resolve is not required—it is not even valid—and mere regret suffices. The author then addresses an objection: 'al-Mawaqif means that mere resolve without regret is not repentance.' The author dismisses this as nonsense, because it fails to explain why ability is mentioned. Footnotes provide information on al-Mawaqif and the definition of laghw (vain speech).

Key Terms
العزم (al-ʿazm)
التوبة (al-tawba)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الندم (al-nadam)
إمام الحرمين (Imam al-Ḥaramayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that repentance always requires a resolve to abandon the sin, even if the person is incapable of committing it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that resolve is only required when the person has the ability to commit the sin; for those incapable, mere regret suffices.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a eunuch not required to resolve to abandon adultery in his repentance?
A) Because a eunuch cannot feel regret.
B) Because resolve is only valid for someone who is capable of committing the sin.
C) Because repentance does not require any inner state.
D) Because the eunuch has already abandoned the sin permanently.

Key Takeaway
When a person is incapable of repeating a sin, their repentance does not require a resolve to abandon it; regret is sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt remorse for a sin you can no longer commit? Did you still burden yourself with an impossible vow to never do it again, even though you cannot? How does this lesson free you from that self-imposed guilt? What step will you take today to accept God's mercy on His terms?
?
Are you secretly trusting in your own resolve to avoid sin, or in God's grace? If you are currently able to commit a sin, have you genuinely resolved to leave it, or do you rely on your own strength? What would change if you truly surrendered your incapacity to God?
?
Imagine you have a sin you deeply regret but know you could fall into again. Does your repentance feel incomplete without a strong resolve? How does the teaching of Sheikh al-Islām reshape your understanding of sincerity? Will you now find peace in regret alone, or still worry your repentance is lacking? What will you do with this freedom?

Critique of the View in al-Mawaqif on Resolve and Ability
The author critiques a view from al-Mawaqif and defends the position that mere regret suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of al-Mawaqif's claim that resolve is not repentance without continued ability, and the correct view that mere regret suffices.

Core Idea
Regret alone, not resolve, is the core of repentance for the incapable.

The author now engages with a statement from the book al-Mawaqif. That book claimed that if a person deprived of power resolves to abandon the sin, it is not repentance. The author argues that this is improper because it implies resolve is valid without ability and that regret plus resolve is insufficient without a third condition. Instead, the correct position is that when ability is absent, resolve is not even valid, and mere regret is enough.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of al-Mawaqif
Identify the point the author objects to. Why does the author say 'not as it should be'?
Pay attention to the implications the author draws.
2
Understand the correct view
The author states that according to Imam al-Haramayn, when there is no power, resolve is not required and even not valid; mere regret suffices.
This is the key doctrinal point.
3
Analyze the objection and rebuttal
The author considers a possible objection that al-Mawaqif meant mere resolve without regret is not repentance. The author dismisses this as nonsense because it fails to explain why ability is mentioned.
Notice the rhetorical dismissal.

يخطر بالبال لذهول أو جنون أو موت ونحو ذلك . وقد لا يقتدر عليه لعارض آفة كخرس في القذف وشلل أوجب في الزنا، فلا يتصور العزم على الترك لما فيه من الإشعار بالقدر والاختيار. فأجيب بأن المراد العزم على الترك على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار ، حتى لو سلب القدرة لم يشترط العزم على الترك. بهذا يشعر كلام إمام الحرمين حيث قال : إن العزم على ترك المعاودة إنما يقارن التوبة في بعض الأحوال، ولا يطرد في كل حال، إذا العزم إنما يصح فيمن يتمكن من مثل ما قدمه ولا يصح من المجبوب العزم على ترك الزنا، ولا من الأخرس العزم على ترك القذف فما ذكر في المواقف (۱) من أن قولنا : إذا قدر لأن من سلب القدرة على الزنا، وانقطع طمعه عن عود القوة إذا عزم على تركه ، لم يكن ذلك توبة منه ، ليس على ما ينبغي لإشعاره بأن العزم على الترك يصح مع عدم القدرة على الفعل وبأن الندم على الفعل مع العزم على الترك لا يكفي في التوبة، لكن لا بد من أمر ثالث هو بقاء القدرة وكلام الإمام وغيره أن عند عدم القدرة لا يشترط في التوبة العزم بل لا يصح ويكفي مجرد الندم . لا يقال : مراد المواقف أن مجرد هذا العزم بدون الندم ليس بتوبة، لأنا نقول : هذا لغو (۲) من الكلام، لا بيان لفائدة التقييد بالقدرة.
(۱) هو كتاب مكون من ثمانية أجزاء في أربعه مجلدات طبع قديما وعليه بعض الحواشي ويعتبر من أهم المراجع في علم الكلام للأشاعرة وصاحبه عضد الدين الايجي يسمى : عبد الرحمن بن أحمد توفي عام ٧٥٦هـ .
(۲) اللغو الباطل، وهو يشتمل الشرك كما قاله بعضهم، والمعاصي كما قاله آخرون، وما لا فائدة فيه من الأقوال والأفعال كما قال تعالى والذين هم عن اللغو معرضون . سورة المؤمنون آية رقم ٢٣ وقال تعالى : (وإذا مروا باللغو مروا كراماً .. سورة الفرقان آية رقم ٧٢
It occurs to the mind due to heedlessness, madness, death, or the like. And one may not be able to commit it due to a defect such as muteness in slander or paralysis that prevents in adultery, so the resolve to abandon is inconceivable because it implies power and choice. So it was answered: what is intended is the resolve to abandon on the estimation of occurrence and capability, so that even if power is removed, the resolve to abandon is not required. This is suggested by the words of Imam al-Haramayn where he said: Verily, the resolve to abandon repetition only accompanies repentance in some states, and is not consistent in every case, because resolve is only valid for one who is able to do something like what he did before, and it is not valid for the eunuch to resolve to abandon adultery, nor for the mute to resolve to abandon slander. So what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif that our saying 'if he is able' because one who is deprived of power over adultery and his hope of returning strength is cut off, if he resolves to abandon it, that is not repentance from him, is not as it should be, because it suggests that resolve to abandon is valid with inability to act, and that regret for the act with resolve to abandon is not sufficient in repentance, but rather a third thing is necessary, which is the continuation of power. And the words of Imam and others indicate that when there is no power, resolve is not required in repentance, rather it is not valid, and mere regret suffices. It should not be said: The meaning of al-Mawaqif is that mere resolve without regret is not repentance, for we say: This is nonsense speech, not an explanation of the benefit of restricting with power.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the role of 'azm (resolve) in tawba (repentance) for a person who has lost the ability to commit the sin (e.g., due to illness, disability, or death). The author begins by noting that a sin may occur to the mind but one may be unable to act due to an impediment. In such cases, requiring a resolve to abandon the sin would suggest that the person has power and choice, which they lack. The answer given is that the intended resolve is on the supposition of the sin occurring and of ability, so even if ability is removed, resolve is not required. The author cites Imam al-Haramayn, who states that resolve to abandon repetition accompanies repentance only in some cases, not always, because resolve is valid only for one who can actually repeat the sin; a eunuch cannot resolve to abandon adultery, nor a mute to abandon slander. The author then critiques a statement in al-Mawaqif which claims that if one deprived of power resolves to abandon, it is not repentance, arguing that this implies resolve is valid without ability and that regret plus resolve is insufficient without a third condition (continued ability). The correct view, according to Imam al-Haramayn, is that when ability is absent, resolve is not required—it is not even valid—and mere regret suffices. The author then addresses an objection: 'al-Mawaqif means that mere resolve without regret is not repentance.' The author dismisses this as nonsense, because it fails to explain why ability is mentioned. Footnotes provide information on al-Mawaqif and the definition of laghw (vain speech).

Key Terms
العزم (al-ʿazm)
التوبة (al-tawba)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الندم (al-nadam)
إمام الحرمين (Imam al-Ḥaramayn)
المواقف (al-Mawāqif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Mawaqif's statement that resolve without continued ability is not repentance is correct.
Correction: The author argues that this view is improper because it implies resolve is valid without ability; the correct view is that mere regret suffices when ability is absent.

Checkpoint
What does the author criticize about the statement from al-Mawaqif?
A) It says resolve is always required.
B) It implies resolve is valid without ability and that regret plus resolve is insufficient without continued ability.
C) It says regret is never sufficient.
D) It ignores the role of intention.

Key Takeaway
The view of al-Mawaqif is criticized for implying resolve is valid without ability; the correct position is that mere regret suffices for repentance when one is incapable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed seeking forgiveness for a sin because you felt you needed to first resolve never to commit it again, even though you knew you were too weak to stop? How does this lesson confront that hesitation?
?
When you are incapable of repeating a sin, do you truly feel the weight of regret, or do you rely on your inability as a substitute for repentance? What would it look like to genuinely repent by regret alone?
?
If mere regret suffices when you are powerless, what does that say about your repentance when you do have the ability? Are you adding resolve or conditions that Allah has not required?

Clarifying that Resolve is for Estimation, Not Restriction
Sanusi clarifies that 'azm is only for estimation and clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mention of resolve in repentance is only for estimation and clarification, and not for restricting the condition of ability.

Core Idea
Resolve in repentance is estimated, not restricted.

This lesson addresses a common misunderstanding about the phrase 'on the estimation of ability' in the definition of repentance. Sanusi clarifies that the mention of resolve is only to indicate what one would do if capable, not to impose a condition on the ability itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's wording guide your understanding.
Notice the technical terms: تقدير (estimation) and تقييد (restriction).
2
Identify the initial misunderstanding
The passage starts with a potential misunderstanding: that the phrase 'on the estimation of ability' restricts the abandonment, not the resolve. What does that mean?
The misunderstanding implies that ability is a condition on actually abandoning sin, not on the resolve to abandon.
3
Understand the correction from al-Mawaqif
The author cites al-Mawaqif to show that even a eunuch must resolve not to commit adultery if he had the ability. This confirms that resolve is linked to estimated ability.
The eunuch example illustrates that resolve is required even when ability is absent.
4
Grasp the final verification
The author concludes that 'azm is only for estimation and clarification, not restriction or precaution. Anyone who truly regrets sin naturally has this resolve.
The phrase 'on the estimation of occurrence and capability' shows the conditional nature of the resolve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتوهم أن تقديره القدرة قيد للترك، لا للعزم . أي يجب العزم على أن لا يفعل على تقدير القدرة حتى يجب على من عرض له الآفة أن يعزم على أن لا يفعل لو فرض وجود القدرة بهذا يشعر ما قال في المواقف أن الزاني المجبوب إذا ندم وعزم على أن لا يعود على تقدير القدرة، فهو توبة عندنا، خلافاً لأبي هاشم . ثم التحقيق أن ذكر العزم إنما للتقدير والبيان لا التقييد والاحتراز. إذ النادم على المعصية لقبحها لا يخرج عن ذلك العزم البتة على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار.
هذا وقد شاع في عرف العوام إطلاق اسم التوبة على الاستئناف وإظهار العزم على ترك المعصية في المستقبل. وليس من التوبة في شيء ما لم يتحقق الندم والأسف على ما مضى، وعلامته طول الحسرة والحزن وانسكاب الدموع. ومن نظر في باب التوبة من كتاب الإحياء للإمام حجة الإسلام، وتأمل فيما يرى من قصة استغفار داود عليه السلام علم صعوبة (أمر التوبة)(۱).
(۱) التوبة النصوح هو توثيق العزم على أن لا يعود لمثله قال ابن عباس - رضي الله عنهما : التوبة النصوح : الندم بالقلب والاستغفار باللسان والاقلاع بالبدن والاضمار على أن لا يعود. وقيل التوبة في اللغة الرجوع عن الذنب وكذلك التوب قال الله تعالى غافر الذنب وقابل التوب، وقيل التوب جمع توبة، والتوبة في الشرع الرجوع عن الأفعال المذمومة إلى الممدوحة، وهي واجبة على الفور عند عامة المسلمين أما الوجوب فلقوله تعالى: ﴿وتوبوا إلى الله جميعاً أيها المؤمنون لعلكم. تفلحون ، وأما الفورية فلما في تأخيرها من الإصرار المحرم ، والإنابة : قريبة من التوبة لغة وشرعاً. والله أعلم.
And it may be misunderstood that his stipulation of ability is a restriction for the abandonment, not for the resolve. That is, it is necessary to resolve not to do [the sin] on the estimation of ability, so that it is incumbent on one who is afflicted with a defect to resolve not to do it if the existence of ability were assumed. This is suggested by what was said in al-Mawaqif: the eunuch adulterer, if he regrets and resolves not to return on the estimation of ability, that is repentance with us, contrary to Abu Hashim. Then the verification is that the mention of resolve is only for estimation and clarification, not for restriction and precaution. For the one who regrets the sin because of its ugliness does not depart from that resolve at all on the estimation of occurrence and capability. This has become widespread in the custom of the common people: applying the name of repentance to making a fresh start and showing resolve to abandon the sin in the future. And it is not repentance at all unless regret and sorrow for what has passed are realized, and its sign is prolonged remorse, grief, and shedding of tears. And whoever looks at the chapter on repentance from the book al-Ihya' of Imam Hujjat al-Islam, and reflects on what he sees of the story of the repentance of David, peace be upon him, knows the difficulty of the matter of repentance.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage clarifies a potential misunderstanding about the role of 'azm (resolve) in tawba (repentance). The author begins by addressing a possible misinterpretation: that the condition of ability (qudra) in the formulation 'on the estimation of ability' is a restriction on the abandonment itself, not on the resolve. The correct understanding, as indicated by al-Mawaqif, is that one must resolve not to commit the sin on the assumption of having the ability, so even a eunuch must resolve not to commit adultery if he were able. The author then states the final verification (tahqiq): the mention of resolve is only for estimation (taqdir) and clarification (bayan), not for restriction (taqyid) or precaution (ihtiraz). Anyone who truly regrets the sin due to its ugliness will naturally have that resolve upon the estimation of ability. Next, the author notes that common people misuse the term tawba to mean merely starting anew and showing future resolve, but true tawba requires genuine regret and sorrow for the past, manifested in prolonged remorse, grief, and tears. He refers to al-Ghazali's Ihya' and the story of David's repentance to emphasize the difficulty of true tawba. The footnote elaborates on tawba nasuh (sincere repentance), defining it as a firm resolve not to return, and quoting Ibn Abbas on the components of sincere repentance (regret, seeking forgiveness, abandoning the sin, and resolve). It also notes the obligation of tawba is immediate.

Key Terms
التقدير (al-taqdīr)
البيان (al-bayān)
التقييد (al-taqyīd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition of ability restricts the abandonment, not the resolve.
Correction: The condition modifies the resolve, not the abandonment; the resolve is only for estimation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of mentioning 'azm (resolve) in the definition of repentance?
A) To restrict the condition of ability.
B) For estimation and clarification.
C) To emphasize the importance of future intentions.
D) To distinguish between different types of sins.

Key Takeaway
Resolve in repentance is only for estimation and clarification, not for restricting the condition of ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you repent from a sin you currently cannot commit (e.g., one you've physically aged out of), do you still resolve never to do it if you had the chance? Or does your lack of ability make your repentance lazy?
?
What does it reveal about your heart if you find yourself making excuses for your repentance—blaming circumstances rather than fully resolving to abandon the sin forever?
?
Do you regret your sins because they are ugly in Allah's sight, or because they brought you worldly trouble? If only the latter, your resolve may fade when the trouble passes. Take a moment to renew your resolve on the estimation of ability, no matter when the opportunity arises.

The True Meaning of Tawba: Regret, Not Just Resolve
Sanusi distinguishes the common and true meanings of tawba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true repentance requires genuine regret and sorrow for past sins, not merely a fresh start and future resolve.

Core Idea
True tawba is regret, not a new beginning.

In common usage, people often equate repentance with simply deciding to stop sinning. But the scholars emphasize that the core of tawba is deep regret and sorrow for what has passed. This lesson examines the distinction and the signs of genuine repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on common custom
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author describes the common people's understanding of tawba.
The common custom is to call any fresh start 'repentance'.
2
Understand the requirement of regret
The author states that true tawba is not realized without regret (nadam) and sorrow (asaf) for the past. Why is regret essential?
Regret is the heart of repentance.
3
Note the signs of true repentance
The passage lists signs: prolonged remorse (harsa), grief (huzn), and shedding of tears (insikab al-dumu'). These are outward indications of inner regret.
These signs show the depth of repentance.
4
Reflect on the example of David
The author refers to al-Ghazali's Ihya' and the story of David's repentance to emphasize the difficulty of true tawba. Reflect on why it is considered difficult.
True repentance requires a complete transformation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the sign that true repentance has occurred?
A) Making a fresh start and saying 'I repent'.
B) Prolonged remorse, grief, and shedding of tears.
C) A firm resolve to never sin again.
D) Simply believing that one has done wrong.

Key Takeaway
True tawba is not a fresh start but genuine regret and sorrow for past sins, shown by prolonged grief and tears.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you have committed and 'repented' from by simply resolving not to do it again. Did you truly feel regret and sorrow for that sin? If not, is your repentance genuine according to this lesson? What would it take for you to experience that sorrow today?
?
The passage says true tawba is marked by prolonged grief, even tears. When was the last time you wept over a sin? If you cannot remember, what might that reveal about the state of your heart? Are you willing to ask Allah to soften your heart?
?
Ibn Abbas describes tawba nasuh as having four components: heart regret, verbal istighfar, physical quitting, and inner resolve. Which of these is weakest in your life? What specific step will you take today to strengthen that component?

Repentance: General Sufficiency and Distinguishing Rights
Sanusi discusses general repentance, refutes Mu'tazila, and distinguishes rights of God vs. people with Imam al-Haramayn's analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why general repentance suffices for all sins and the additional obligations required when sins involve the rights of others.

Core Idea
General repentance suffices, but sins against others demand restitution beyond regret.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a practical question: do we need to repent for each sin individually? It defends general repentance and then distinguishes between obligations when the sin is purely against God versus when it involves the rights of people. Imam al-Haramayn's insight on murder versus usurpation clarifies when repentance is valid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the debate on general vs. specific repentance
Read how the passage states that general repentance suffices for all sins, even if known in detail. Note the Mu'tazila objection requiring detailed regret.
The refutation uses the principle that obligations cannot be beyond capacity.
2
Grasp the refutation of the Mu'tazila argument
The passage argues that since one must repent at all times, but cannot accumulate many sins at once, requiring detailed repentance would be an unbearable obligation (taklif ma la yutaq).
3
Distinguish between sins against God and sins against people
Notice the two categories: for purely God-related sins, regret may suffice or an extra action (e.g., surrendering for hadd) may be needed. For people-related sins, regret must be accompanied by restoring the right, guiding if misguidance, or apologizing if harm.
Backbiting does not require detailing what was said unless it was uglier.
4
Learn the verification on murder vs. usurpation
Imam al-Haramayn's example shows that for murder, repentance is valid without surrendering for retribution (the prevention is a new sin). For usurpation, repentance is invalid without returning the property because regret cannot coexist with continued possession.
This highlights the difference between obligations that can be separate and those that are integral to repentance.

واجب آخر. إلا أنه قد لا يصح الندم بدونه ، كرد المغصوب). يعني يكفي التوبة عن المعاصي كلها الإجمال، وإن علمت مفصلة لحصول الندم والعزم . وذهب بعض المعتزلة إلى أنه لا بد من الندم تفصيلا فيما علم مفصلاً . ورد بأنه مكلف بالتوبة في كل وقت مع امتناع اجتماع الذنوب الكثيرة في وقت واحد. فلو لم يكف الإجمال، لزم تكليف ما لا يطاق. قالوا : ثم إن كانت المعصية في خالص حق الله تعالى كالواجب، فقد يكفي الندم، كما في ارتكاب الفرار من الزحف وترك الأمر بالمعروف. وقد يفتقر إلى أمر زائد كتسليم النفس للحد في الشرب، وتسليم ما وجب في ترك الزكاة، ومثله في ترك الصلاة وإن تعلقت بحقوق العباد لزم مع الندم إيصال حق العبد ، أو بدله إليه إن كان الذنب ظلماً كما في الغصب، والقتل العمد ولزم إرشاده إن كان الذنب إضلالاً له والاعتذار إليه إن كان إيذاء كما في الغيبة . ولا يلزم تفصيل ما اغتابه به إلا إذا بلغه على وجه أفحش . والتحقيق أن هذا الزائد واجب آخر خارج عن التوبة على ما قال إمام الحرمين أن القاتل إذا ندم من غير تسليم نفسه للقصاص، صحت توبته في حق الله تعالى، وكان منعه القصاص من مستحقه معصية متجددة تستدعي توبة. ولا يقدح في التوبة عن القتل . ثم قال : وربما لا يصح التوبة بدون الخروج من حق العبد كما في الغصب ، فإنه لا يصح الندم عليه مع إدامة اليد على المغصوب ففرق بين القتل والغصب.
Another obligation. However, regret may not be valid without it, like returning the usurped property. Meaning, general repentance suffices for all sins, even if known in detail, because regret and resolve are achieved. And some Mu'tazila held that detailed regret is necessary for what is known in detail. And it was refuted that he is obligated to repent at all times while it is impossible for many sins to occur at one time. So if general repentance were not sufficient, it would entail an obligation beyond capacity. They said: Then if the sin is purely regarding the right of God Almighty, like an obligatory act, regret may suffice, as in committing flight from battle and abandoning enjoining good. And it may require an additional matter, such as surrendering oneself for the prescribed penalty in drinking, and surrendering what was obligatory for abandoning zakat, and similarly for abandoning prayer. And if it pertains to the rights of people, then along with regret, delivering the right of the person or its substitute to him is necessary if the sin was oppression, as in usurpation and intentional murder. And guidance is necessary if the sin was misguidance for him, and apology if it was harm, as in backbiting. And it is not necessary to detail what he backbit about unless he told him in an uglier way. And the verification is that this additional matter is another obligation outside of repentance, according to what Imam al-Haramayn said: that if the killer regrets without surrendering himself for retribution, his repentance is valid regarding the right of God Almighty, and his preventing retribution from its due is a renewed sin requiring repentance, and it does not harm the repentance for the murder. Then he said: And perhaps repentance is not valid without exiting from the right of the person, as in usurpation, for remorse is not valid while continuing to hold the usurped property. So there is a difference between murder and usurpation.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions of repentance (tawba). It begins by stating that there is an additional obligation beyond regret, such as returning usurped property. It then clarifies that general repentance (al-tawba al-ijmālī) suffices for all sins, even if known in detail, because regret and resolve are present. It refutes the Mu'tazila who require detailed repentance for known sins, arguing that one is obligated to repent at all times while it is impossible to accumulate many sins at once, so general repentance must suffice to avoid unbearable obligations (taklif ma la yutaq). Then it distinguishes between sins that are purely against the rights of God (khāliṣ ḥaqq Allāh), where regret may suffice (e.g., fleeing from battle, abandoning enjoining good), but sometimes an additional action is needed (e.g., surrendering for hadd penalty or paying missed obligations). For sins involving the rights of people (ḥuqūq al-‘ibād), along with regret, one must deliver the right or its substitute if the sin was oppression (ẓulm), provide guidance if the sin was misguidance (iḍlāl), or apologize if the sin was harm (īdhā’) like backbiting. The passage then gives a verification (taḥqīq) quoting Imam al-Haramayn: for murder, regret without surrendering for retribution is valid regarding God's right, but the refusal creates a new sin; for usurpation, repentance is invalid without returning the property because regret cannot coexist with continued possession. Thus there is a distinction between murder and usurpation.

Key Terms
توبة
ندم
إجمال
تفصيل
حق الله
حق العبد
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance from murder requires surrendering yourself for retribution; otherwise it is invalid.
Correction: According to Imam al-Haramayn, regret alone makes repentance valid regarding God's right. The refusal to surrender is a new sin, but it does not invalidate the original repentance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does general repentance suffice for sins known in detail?
A) Because detailed repentance is impossible
B) Because one is obligated to repent at all times and cannot accumulate many sins at once, so general repentance prevents unbearable obligation
C) Because regret and resolve are achieved only in general repentance
D) Because the Mu'tazila require it

Key Takeaway
General repentance suffices for all sins, but sins against others require restoring their rights; repentance without restitution is valid for murder but not for usurpation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you have committed that involved harming another person, even if only through words. Have you sincerely apologized and made amends, or have you assumed that your general repentance covers it? What specific step will you take before tonight's prayer to begin making it right?
?
The passage says that for murder, repentance is valid regarding God's right even without surrendering for retribution. But it also says preventing retribution is a separate sin. Is there an area in your life where you claim to have repented from a major sin but continue to allow its consequences to persist unfairly? What is that area, and when will you fully surrender to justice?
?
Imam al-Haramayn's analysis shows that some obligations are 'outside repentance'—they are separate duties. Have you been conflating your private regret with the public or interpersonal obligations that remain? What one relationship needs repair that you have been neglecting because you think your repentance alone suffices?

The Obligation of Commanding Good and Forbidding Evil
Sanusi defines the obligation, terms, and proofs, and refutes the Rafida view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil, including its precise definitions, the proofs from Quran and Sunnah, and the refutation of the Rafida claim that it is conditional on the Imam's appearance.

Core Idea
Commanding good and forbidding evil is an obligation on every Muslim, not contingent on any ruler.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid establishes one of the most important collective duties in Islam: commanding good and forbidding evil. The author, al-Sanusi, begins by defining the key terms and then provides evidence from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. He also addresses a common misconception held by the Rafida (a Shi'a group) that this duty only applies after the appearance of the infallible Imam. Let's dive into the text to understand the scope and basis of this obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has a clear structure: definition, explanation, proof, and refutation.
2
Understand the definitions
The passage specifies that 'good' (al-ma'ruf) here means the obligatory, and 'evil' (al-munkar) means the forbidden. This precision is important because commanding recommended acts is not obligatory.
Contrast: commanding the recommended (mandub) is recommended, not obligatory.
3
Examine the proofs from Quran and Sunnah
Identify the Quranic verses (3:104, 31:17) and the three hadiths cited. Notice how each proof establishes obligation.
The hadith about degrees of changing evil by hand, tongue, and heart is especially well-known.
4
Understand the refutation of the Rafida
The passage explicitly rejects the idea that commanding good and forbidding evil depends on the appearance of an Imam. The proofs show it is a general obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس عشر - قد أطبق الكتاب والسنة والإجماع على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر. فالمراد بالمعروف الواجب، والمنكر الحرام وإلا فالأمر بالمندوب، أو النهي عن المكروه ليس بواجب، بل مندوب . وقوله تعالى: (عليكم أنفسكم لا يضركم من ضل إذا اهتديتم ) معناه : أصلحوا أنفسكم لأداء الواجبات، وترك المعاصي وبالأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر لا يضركم بعد النهي عنادهم وإصرارهم، ولا إكراه في الدين منسوخ بآيات القتال ورخصة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) في الترك إنما عند انتفاء الشرط، وهو العلم بوجه المعروف والمنكر بتجويز التأثير وانتفاء المفسدة) . هي في الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر. قد جرت عادة المتكلمين بإيرادهما في علم الكلام مع أنهما بالفروع أشبه ، وكأنهما يشبهان التوبة في الزجر عن ارتكاب المعصية والإخلال بالواجب والمراد بالمعروف الواجب، وبالمنكر الحرام، ولهذا بتوا القول بأنهما واجبان مع القطع بأن الأمر بالمندوب ليس بواجب بل مندوب والدليل على وجوبهما من غير توقف على ظهور الإمام كما يزعم الروافض الكتاب والسنة والإجماع . أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى : ولتكن منكم أمة يدعون إلى الخير ويأمرون بالمعروف وينهون عن المنكر ) . وقوله تعالى : وأمر بالمعروف وانه عن المنكر . وأما السنة فلقوله (عليه السلام) مر بالمعروف، وانه عن المنكر، واصبر على ما أصابك» . وقوله (عليه السلام) لتأمرن بالمعروف ولتنهون عن المنكر، أو ليسلطن الله عليكم شراركم ، ثم يدعوا خياركم فلا يستجاب لكم . وقوله (عليه السلام) من رأى منكم منكراً فليغيره بيده. فإن لم يستطع، فبلسانه . فإن لم يستطع فبقلبه. وهذا أضعف الإيمان».
The fifteenth investigation – The Book, the Sunnah, and consensus have affirmed the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil. What is meant by good is the obligatory, and by evil is the forbidden; otherwise, commanding the recommended or forbidding the disliked is not obligatory, but recommended. And His statement, the Exalted: 'Upon you yourselves; he who goes astray will not harm you if you are guided' means: Reform yourselves to perform obligations and abandon sins, and by commanding good and forbidding evil, you will not be harmed after the prohibition of their obstinacy and persistence. And 'There is no compulsion in religion' is abrogated by the verses of fighting, and the Prophet's (peace be upon him) permission to abandon [it] is only when the condition is absent, which is knowledge of the aspect of good and evil by allowing effectiveness and absence of corruption. It is in commanding good and forbidding evil. The custom of the theologians has been to include them in the science of kalam even though they are more akin to the branches, and they resemble repentance in deterring from committing sin and neglecting what is obligatory. And what is meant by good is the obligatory, and by evil is the forbidden, therefore they decisively state that both are obligatory, while being certain that commanding the recommended is not obligatory but recommended. The proof for their obligation without depending on the appearance of the Imam, as the Rafida claim, is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book, it is His statement: 'Let there be from you a nation inviting to good, commanding good, and forbidding evil' and His statement: 'Command good and forbid evil.' As for the Sunnah, it is his statement (peace be upon him): 'Command good, forbid evil, and be patient with what afflicts you' and his statement (peace be upon him): 'You will certainly command good and forbid evil, or Allah will indeed set over you the worst of you, then your best will call but it will not be answered' and his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever among you sees an evil, let him change it with his hand; if he is not able, then with his tongue; if he is not able, then with his heart, and that is the weakest of faith.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fifteenth investigation (al-mabḥath al-khāmis ‘ashar) on commanding good and forbidding evil. It begins by asserting the obligation (wujūb) based on the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus (ijmā‘). It defines the terms: 'good' (al-ma‘rūf) means the obligatory (al-wājib), and 'evil' (al-munkar) means the forbidden (al-ḥarām); thus commanding recommended acts or forbidding disliked acts is not obligatory but recommended. It then explains the Quranic verse (5:105): 'Upon you yourselves; he who goes astray will not harm you if you are guided' – meaning that if you reform yourselves by performing obligations and abandoning sins, and you engage in commanding good and forbidding evil, the persistence of others in misguidance will not harm you. It notes that the verse 'There is no compulsion in religion' (2:256) is abrogated (mansūkh) by the verses of fighting (āyāt al-qitāl), and the Prophet's permission to refrain from commanding and forbidding is conditional on the absence of the required conditions: knowledge of the aspect of good and evil, likelihood of effectiveness, and absence of greater harm. The author remarks that theologians customarily include this topic in kalam despite it being more akin to fiqh (subsidiary matters), because it resembles repentance in deterring from sin and fulfilling obligations. Then the proof of obligation is given from the Quran (3:104 and 31:17) and from the Sunnah with three hadiths: the first commands to command good and forbid evil with patience; the second warns that if Muslims abandon this duty, Allah will set evil rulers over them and will not answer the prayers of the righteous; the third outlines the degrees of changing evil – by hand, tongue, or heart – with the heart being the weakest of faith. The passage also refutes the Rafida (Shi‘a) claim that commanding and forbidding are only obligatory after the appearance of the Imam, asserting that its obligation does not depend on that.

النهي عن المنكر
معروف

مندوب
مكروه
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding good and forbidding evil is only obligatory after the appearance of the infallible Imam (as claimed by the Rafida/Shi'a).
Correction: The passage proves the obligation is unconditional, based on the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, and does not depend on any Imam's appearance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do 'ma'ruf' and 'munkar' refer to in the context of the obligation?
A) Any good and any evil
B) The obligatory and the forbidden respectively
C) The recommended and the disliked respectively
D) All actions that are pleasing or displeasing to God

Key Takeaway
The obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil is established by clear texts from Quran and Sunnah, and it does not depend on the presence of an infallible Imam; it is a duty for all Muslims according to their ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an evil, do you truly assess the conditions of knowledge, effectiveness, and harm, or do you use fear of discomfort as an excuse to remain silent? What concrete step will you take this week to fulfill this obligation?
?
The hadith warns that if Muslims abandon commanding good and forbidding evil, Allah will set evil rulers over them. Look at the state of the Muslim world: do you see a connection? What is your personal responsibility in this light?
?
Are you quick to command good and forbid evil in private matters (like with family) but hesitant in public? The proof is unconditional. What is the one area of your life where you know you must speak but have been silent? Commit to addressing it by next prayer time.

The Subject of Names and Judgments
Explains the technical meaning of 'names' and 'judgments' in classical theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what early scholars meant by 'names' and 'judgments' in the context of Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Names classify believers and sinners; judgments determine their eternal fate.

The third chapter of this work concerns a topic central to Islamic theology: how we name people based on their faith and actions, and what those names imply for the afterlife. Early scholars had a specific usage for the terms 'names' and 'judgments' that differs from our common understanding. This passage clarifies that usage and sets the stage for the detailed discussions ahead.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's explanation.
The passage is short and direct.
2
Identify the two categories
Note that 'names' refer to appellations of praise and blame, and 'judgments' to their eschatological consequences.
Look for examples given.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this framework is essential for understanding later debates on faith, sin, and salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثالث في الأسماء والأحكام: وفيه مباحث . هذه الترجمة شائعة في كلام المتقدمين، ويعنون بالأسماء أسامي المكلفين في المدح، مثل المؤمن والمسلم، والمتقي، والصالح، وفي الذم مثل الكافر والفاسق والمنافق . وبالأحكام ما لكل منها في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب وكيفيتهما . قال : المبحث الأول - الإيمان في اللغة التصديق، إفعال من الأمن للصيرورة أو التعدية . ويعدى بالباء واللام لملاحظة معنى الاعتراف والإذعان، ولما أن مآله إلى أخذ الشيء صادقاً، والصدق مما يوصف به المتكلم، والكلام والحكم تعلق بالشيء باعتبارات مختلفة مثل : آمنت بالله وبالملائكة، وبالكتاب، وبالرسول، وباليوم
He said: The third chapter on Names and Judgments. It contains investigations. This title is common in the speech of the early scholars, and by 'names' they mean the appellations of the obligated persons in praise, such as believer, Muslim, pious, righteous, and in dispraise such as disbeliever, sinner, hypocrite. And by 'judgments' they mean what each of them will have in the Hereafter of reward and punishment and their modalities. He said: The first investigation: Faith linguistically is affirmation (tasdiq), a verbal form from al-amn (security) for becoming or transitivity. And it is made transitive with ba' and lam in consideration of the meaning of acknowledgment and submission, and because its outcome is to take something as true, and truth is something attributed to the speaker, and speech and judgment relate to a thing from different perspectives, such as: I believed in God, in the angels, in the Book, in the Messenger, and in the Day...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter titled 'On Names and Judgments' (al-asma' wa al-ahkam). It explains that this title was common among early scholars: by 'names' they mean the terms used to praise or blame responsible beings, such as believer, Muslim, pious, righteous (praise), and disbeliever, sinner, hypocrite (blame). By 'judgments' they mean the rewards and punishments in the Hereafter and their qualities. Then it moves to the first investigation: the linguistic definition of 'iman' (faith). It states that iman linguistically is tasdiq (affirmation), derived from the root amn (security) as a verbal form indicating becoming or transitivity. The verb is used with the prepositions ba' and lam, implying acknowledgment and submission, and that the object is taken as true. Truth is a quality of the speaker, and speech and judgment relate to things from different perspectives. Examples are given: 'I believed in God, in the angels, in the Book, in the Messenger, and in the Day...' (the sentence is cut off).

Key Terms
الأسماء
الأحكام
المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do early scholars mean by 'judgments' (al-ahkam)?
A) The legal rulings of Sharia.
B) The rewards and punishments in the Hereafter for each name.
C) The names of the books of Hadith.
D) The attributes of God.

Key Takeaway
Early scholars used the term 'names' to categorize individuals by their religious status and 'judgments' to indicate their eternal outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the names mentioned in the passage—believer, Muslim, pious, righteous, disbeliever, sinner, hypocrite—do you truly believe describes you right now? If you are honest, which one fits your current state of faith and action?
?
The passage teaches that every name carries a specific eternal judgment. Does your daily life reflect that you are living as if your name will determine your reward or punishment? What would need to change if you took this connection seriously?
?
If you knew that your current religious label (e.g., 'Muslim') is being weighed against your actual deeds, what is the one thing you would commit to changing today to align your name with your actions?

Linguistic Definition of Iman (Faith)
Defines 'iman' linguistically as tasdiq (affirmation) and explains its derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic linguistic meaning of 'iman' is 'tasdiq' (affirmation), and how this relates to its verbal form and usage.

Core Idea
Faith linguistically is sincere affirmation of the heart.

Having explained the chapter's subject, the author now turns to the first investigation: the linguistic definition of iman (faith). This is crucial because theological discussions about faith often begin from its linguistic roots. The passage explains that iman is derived from the root 'amn' (security) and means 'tasdiq' (affirmation), and it is used with certain prepositions to convey acknowledgment and submission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the definition.
Note the key term 'tasdiq'.
2
Understand the derivation
Note that 'iman' is a verbal form from 'amn' indicating becoming or transitivity.
This is a morphological point.
3
Note the prepositions
Observe that it is made transitive with 'ba' and 'lam' to imply acknowledgment and submission.
Examples are given.

D) Sincerity (ikhlas).

Key Takeaway
Linguistically, iman means tasdiq (affirmation), derived from the root 'amn' and used with prepositions to imply acknowledgment and submission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly affirm the existence and attributes of Allah in your heart, or does your tongue sometimes speak while your heart remains distant?
?
When you say 'I believe in the angels, the books, the messengers', is it a mere recitation or does it come from deep acknowledgment and submission?
?
In what areas of your life does your faith lack the tasdiq that the Arabic root implies? How can you strengthen that inner affirmation today?
